2:25:1422:0:14:71:373:700:12:Times:12:Times:?:Job Search Behavior among the Employed and Non-Employed
2:26:1477:14:14:71:427:686:12:Times:12:Times:?:R.Jason Faberman, Andreas I. Mueller, Ayşegül Şahin, and Giorgio Topa
2:27:1546:14:14:71:233:672:12:Times:12:Times:?:NBER Working Paper No. 23731
2:28:1574:14:14:71:133:658:12:Times:12:Times:?:August 2017

2:30:1604:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:A:ABSTRACT
2:31:1612:28:14:72:546:588:12:Times:12:Times:?:Using  a  unique  new  survey,  we  study  the  relationship  between  search  effort  and  outcomes  for 
2:32:1718:14:14:72:543:574:12:Times:12:Times:?:employed and  non-employed  workers.  We  find  that  the  employed  fare  better  than  the  non-
2:33:1815:14:14:71:545:560:12:Times:12:Times:?:employed in job search: they receive more offers per application and are offered higher pay even 
2:34:1911:14:14:71:545:546:12:Times:12:Times:?:after   controlling   for   observable characteristics.   We   use   an   on-the-job   search   model   with 
2:35:2019:14:14:71:545:532:12:Times:12:Times:?:endogenous search effort and find that unobserved heterogeneity explains less than a third of the 
2:36:2116:14:14:72:546:518:12:Times:12:Times:?:residual  wage  offer  differential.  The  model  calibrated using  various  moments  from  our  survey 
2:37:2219:14:42:72:481:504:12:Times:12:Times:?:provides a good fit to the data and implies a reasonable flow value of unemployment.
2:38:2302:42:14:72:164:462:12:Times:12:Times:?:R. Jason Faberman
2:39:2319:14:14:72:227:448:12:Times:12:Times:?:Economic Research Department

3:63:2811:0:36:81:194:709:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:1    Introduction
3:64:2828:36:25:81:529:673:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Job-to-job transitions are an important feature of the U.S. labor market. Fallick and Fleischman
3:65:2924:25:25:81:529:648:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(2004)  show  that,  at  the  end  of  the  1990s,  the  monthly  job-to-job  transition  rate  was  2.6
3:66:3028:25:25:81:530:623:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent, which accounted for around 40 percent of all monthly hiring.1Furthermore, job-to-
3:67:3118:25:25:81:529:598:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job transitions are an important driver of aggregate wage and productivity growth (Faberman
3:68:3209:25:25:81:458:573:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and Justiniano, 2015; Moscarini and Postel-Vinay, 2017; Karahan et al., 2017).
3:69:3287:25:25:98:529:548:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Despite  the  critical  importance  of  on-the-job  search  for  understanding  labor  market  dy-
3:70:3385:25:25:81:529:523:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:namics  and  the  central  role  it  takes  in  search  theories  of  the  labor  market,  evidence  on  the
3:71:3493:25:24:81:530:498:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:extent and nature of the on-the-job search remains scant.2In this paper, we help fill this void
3:72:3588:24:25:81:529:474:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:by documenting the search behavior and outcomes of employed and non-employed individuals
3:73:3676:25:25:81:524:449:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and assessing the importance of our findings for search-theoretic models of the labor market.
3:74:3769:25:25:98:529:424:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:To  this  end,  we  design  and  implement  a  unique  new  survey  that  focuses  on  job  search
3:75:3867:25:25:81:530:399:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:behavior and outcomes forallindividuals, regardless of their labor force status.  Our survey
3:76:3959:25:25:81:529:374:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is  administered  as  a  supplement  to  the  Survey  of  Consumer  Expectations,  which  is  fielded
3:77:4060:25:25:81:529:349:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:monthly by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York to a sample of roughly 1,300 individuals.
3:78:4149:25:25:81:529:324:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Our supplement has been fielded annually each October since 2013.  It asks an expansive list of
3:79:4244:25:25:81:529:299:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:questions on the employment status and current job search, if any, of all respondents.  We ask
3:80:4338:25:25:81:529:274:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about an individual’s search effort and search methods, including whether any unsolicited con-
3:81:4432:25:24:81:529:249:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tacts, referrals, or other informal methods were involved.  We also elicit extensive information
3:82:4528:24:25:81:529:225:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on worker and job characteristics.  For search outcomes, we ask about any job offers received,
3:83:4622:25:16:81:529:200:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:how those offers came about, the characteristics of those offers, and whether these offers were
3:84:4717:16:16:94:530:184:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:1More  recently,  the  job-to-job  transition  rate  has  declined  (Davis  and  Haltiwanger,  2014),  but  so  has  the
3:85:4837:16:12:81:529:168:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:transition rate from unemployment to employment,  so job-to-job transitions remain an important fraction of
3:86:4944:12:8:81:251:156:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:aggregate hiring in the U.S. labor market.
3:87:4986:8:16:94:530:148:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:2Notable  exceptions  are  earlier  work  by  Blau  and  Robins  (1990)  and  Holzer  (1987).   As  we  detail  in  the
3:88:5105:16:12:81:529:132:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:empirical section, these studies are based on older, discontinued surveys.  Researchers have used the more recent
3:89:5218:12:12:81:529:120:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:American  Time  Use  Survey  (ATUS)  extensively  to  analyze  the  job  search  behavior  of  the  unemployed  (see
3:90:5334:12:12:81:529:108:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:Krueger and Mueller,  2010,  Aguiar,  Hurst,  and Karabourbanis,  2013,  and Mukoyama,  Patterson,  and S ̧ahin,
3:91:5446:12:12:81:529:96:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:2017),  but the ATUS is not well-suited to analyze the job search effort of the employed as we discuss in the
3:92:5555:12:0:81:151:84:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:empirical section.
4:93:5574:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:accepted.  We also ask those who are currently employed similar questions about the search
4:94:5664:25:25:81:255:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:process that led to their current job.
4:95:5702:25:25:98:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Our findings provide the most comprehensive evidence to date on the nature of on-the-job
4:96:5790:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search for the U.S. On-the-job search is pervasive, with 23 percent of the employed looking for
4:97:5885:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:work during our survey months.  The overarching theme from our analysis is that the employed
4:98:5977:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:face relatively better job search prospects along multiple dimensions.  With regard to theories
4:99:6072:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of labor market search, two margins in particular stand out.  First, the search process of the
4:100:6166:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed appears to be more efficient than that of the unemployed.  The unemployed exert
4:101:6254:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:nearly twice as much search effort as employed job seekers but generate fewer employer contacts
4:102:6349:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and about the same number of job offers compared to the employed.  In addition, employed
4:103:6437:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:workers who report no measurable search activity receive over one-quarter of all offers in our
4:104:6531:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:sample, underscoring the importance of informal recruiting mechanisms for generating differ-
4:105:6623:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ences in search efficiency by labor force status.3Second, the employed appear to sample from
4:106:6715:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a higher-quality job offer distribution than the non-employed.  This holds even after control-
4:107:6809:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ling for observable characteristics of the worker and the job.  When the non-employed receive
4:108:6902:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a job offer, we find that it tends to pay a lower wage, offer fewer hours, and is considerably
4:109:6996:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:less likely to offer any benefits.  Unconditionally,  the wages offered to the non-employed are
4:110:7091:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:40 log points (49 percent) lower than the wages offered to the employed.  After accounting for
4:111:7185:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:observable characteristics, the average wage offered to the non-employed remains 25 log points
4:112:7279:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(28 percent) lower.  Despite the poor quality of these job offers, the non-employed are about
4:113:7372:25:25:81:313:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:one-and-a-half times more likely to accept them.
4:114:7420:25:24:98:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The finding that employed workers face better wage offers suggests that factors that are
4:115:7508:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unique to employment status are important determinants of the hiring process.  An obvious
4:116:7597:25:16:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:concern about this interpretation, however, is that unobserved differences in productivity be-
4:117:7691:16:16:94:530:120:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:3This finding has strong implications for labor market models that incorporate on-the-job search and analo-
4:118:7798:16:12:81:529:104:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:gous to recent work on vacancies by Davis, Faberman, and Haltiwanger (2013), who find that a sizable fraction
4:119:7907:12:0:81:329:92:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:of hiring by firms occurs without the use of a formal vacancy.
5:120:7970:0:25:81:530:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f6:B:tween employed and non-employed job seekers may be the reason for what appears to be awage
5:121:8060:25:25:81:530:684:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:offer premium.  To assess the role of heterogeneity in our data more systematically, we set up an
5:122:8157:25:25:81:530:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on-the-job search model with unobservedex anteheterogeneity in worker productivity.  We dis-
5:123:8249:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:cipline the differences in unobserved productivity in our model with data on individuals’ wages
5:124:8344:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for the job held prior to their current job.  The model simulation suggests that low-productivity
5:125:8441:25:25:81:530:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:workers are negatively selected into unemployment.4Nevertheless, the negative selection only
5:126:8533:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:accounts for 28 percent (7 log points) of the 25 log point residual wage offer differential.  We
5:127:8629:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:also provide evidence that controlling for the fraction of time employed over the last 5 years
5:128:8723:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(in addition to the standard observables) reduces the unexplained wage offer premium only to
5:129:8815:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a limited extent, but in similar magnitude in the data and the model.  This suggests that our
5:130:8908:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model—which, for the purpose of identification, exploits prior wages rather than employment
5:131:8999:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:histories as a source of variation—captures the unobserved heterogeneity that is relevant for
5:132:9092:25:25:81:298:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the empirically-observed wage offer premium.
5:133:9136:25:25:98:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The model also allows us to relate our findings to the quantitative implications of search
5:134:9226:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:theory.  We base our framework on the model of Christensen et al.  (2005), and—in addition
5:135:9316:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to incorporating worker heterogeneity—extend it along several dimensions.  First, we allow for
5:136:9410:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:differences in search costs and search efficiency by employment status.  Search efficiency in the
5:137:9507:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model captures differences in the job-offer arrival rate (per unit of search effort) between the
5:138:9603:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed  and  unemployed  that  we  observe  in  our  survey.   In  practice,  these  differences  can
5:139:9706:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:occur for a variety of reasons, including employer preferences and differences in the incidence
5:140:9801:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of unsolicited employer contacts and referrals.  When fed into the model, our findings on search
5:141:9897:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effort  and  search  outcomes  suggest  that  the  employed  are  nearly  three  times  more  efficient
5:142:10000:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in their job search than the unemployed.  Second, we allow for wage offers to be censored to
5:143:10092:25:16:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:account for the possibility that employed job seekers are more selective in the pre-offer stage of
5:144:10190:16:16:94:530:120:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:4Our model estimates are consistent with Mueller (2017) who finds in the Current Population Survey that
5:145:10293:16:12:81:529:104:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:unemployment risk is 36 percent higher for workers below the median residual wage (i.e., after controlling for
5:146:10403:12:0:81:341:92:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:observables) compared to those above the median residual wage.
6:147:10466:0:25:81:530:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f6:B:the search process.  We calibrate the degree of censoring using specific questions onunrealized
6:148:10561:25:25:81:530:684:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:offersin our survey.  To the extent that the employed are more selective and reject or avoid
6:149:10653:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offers before they are formally made, this magnifies their underlying search efficiency.  Finally,
6:150:10751:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we extend our model to allow for differences in the wage offer distributions of the employed
6:151:10843:25:25:81:471:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and unemployed that are independent of censoring and unobserved heterogeneity.
6:152:10921:25:25:98:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:All three model extensions improve the fit to our data.  The full version of our model also
6:153:11012:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:implies a reasonable flow value of unemployment of 0.74, passing a key test advocated by Horn-
6:154:11106:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:stein, Krusell, and Violante (2011).  These authors show that standard search models (such as
6:155:11199:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in McCall, 1970, Mortensen, 1977, Pissarides, 1985) exhibit a tension between observed worker
6:156:11292:25:25:81:530:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f6:B:flows, wage dispersion and the flow value of unemployment (also known as thefrictional wage
6:157:11383:25:24:81:530:459:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:dispersionpuzzle).  The tension arises because high dispersion in potential wage offers generates
6:158:11480:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a large option value of waiting for a better offer, so only a low (often negative) flow value of
6:159:11576:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployment can rationalize the observed flow rate of the unemployed into employment.  In
6:160:11666:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:our model, the option value of unemployment is limited since employed searchers are relatively
6:161:11760:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:efficient in generating offers and face a better wage offer distribution.  Therefore, leaving un-
6:162:11857:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employment for a job does not require unemployment to have a low flow value.  Our empirical
6:163:11948:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:findings  thus  constitute  an  important  piece  in  the  solution  of  the  frictional  wage  dispersion
6:164:12054:25:25:81:114:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:puzzle.
6:165:12061:25:25:98:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:An open question remains for why wage offers are of better quality for the employed.  While
6:166:12152:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we  do  not  model  the  many  potential  micro-foundations  for  the  wage  offer  differential,  but
6:167:12254:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:simply calibrate an exogenous differential to match the empirically observed one, the reduced
6:168:12347:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:form is consistent with auction models where incumbent and outside employers compete for
6:169:12435:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:workers  and  bid  up  the  wages  of  the  employed  (Postel-Vinay  and  Robin,  2002,  and  Cahuc,
6:170:12535:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Postel-Vinay and Robin, 2006).  It is also consistent with employer discrimination against the
6:171:12629:25:25:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed, either to take advantage of their lower reservation wages (as in Carrillo-Tudela,
6:172:12722:25:0:81:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:2009)  or  through  a  stigma  effect  (as  in  Gibbons  and  Katz,  1991).   Finally,  the  wage  offer
7:173:12827:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:differential may be the result of the employed having access to networks that provide better
7:174:12919:25:25:81:128:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job offers.
7:175:12930:25:25:98:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The next section describes our survey.  Section 3 presents our evidence on job search be-
7:176:13019:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:havior and job search outcomes by labor force status.  Section 4 presents a model of on-the-job
7:177:13114:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search  with endogenous  search  effort and  its calibration  to our  key  findings.  Section 5  con-
7:178:13215:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:cludes and offers some additional thoughts on the mechanisms underlying the observed wage
7:179:13304:25:36:81:414:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offer differential between the employed and non-employed job seekers.
7:180:13373:36:36:81:278:523:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:2    Survey Design and Data
7:181:13400:36:25:81:529:487:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Our data come from a supplement to the Survey of Consumer Expectations (SCE), administered
7:182:13490:25:25:81:529:462:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York.  The SCE is a monthly nationally-representative
7:183:13578:25:25:81:529:437:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:survey of roughly 1,300 individuals that asks respondents about their expectations about vari-
7:184:13672:25:24:81:529:412:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ous aspects of the economy.  We designed the supplement ourselves and first administered it in
7:185:13766:24:25:81:529:388:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:October 2013.  We have administered it annually since then, and present results for a sample
7:186:13858:25:25:81:529:363:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that  pools  the  2013-15  data  together.   Our  supplement  asks  a  broad  range  of  questions  on
7:187:13960:25:25:81:529:338:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employment status, job search behavior, and job search outcomes.  Demographic data are also
7:188:14051:25:25:81:433:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:available for respondents through the monthly portion of the SCE survey.
7:189:14123:25:25:98:529:288:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The  survey  asks  a  variety  of  questions  that  are  tailored  to  an  individual’s  employment
7:190:14222:25:25:81:529:263:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status and job search behavior.  For the employed, including the self-employed, the survey asks
7:191:14317:25:25:81:529:238:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:questions  about  their  wages,  hours,  benefits,  and  the  type  of  work  that  they  do,  including
7:192:14421:25:25:81:529:213:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:questions on the characteristics of their workplace.  For the non-employed, the survey asks a
7:193:14514:25:25:81:529:188:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:range of detailed questions on their most recent employment spell and their reasons for non-
7:194:14606:25:24:81:529:163:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employment.  The survey also asks questions related to the type of non-employment, including
7:195:14698:24:25:81:529:139:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:those  related  to  retirement,  school  enrollment  status,  and  any  temporary  layoff.   We  also
7:196:14799:25:25:81:529:114:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ask individuals about their prior work history.  This includes detailed information about the
7:197:14892:25:0:81:274:89:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:preceding job of the currently employed.
8:198:14933:0:25:98:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Regardless of employment status, the survey asks all individuals if they have searched for
8:199:15023:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:work within the last four weeks, and if they had not searched, whether or not they would accept
8:200:15118:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a job if one was offered to them.  Among the employed, the survey distinguishes between those
8:201:15211:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:searching  for  new  work  and  those  searching  for  a  job  in  addition  to  their  current  one.   For
8:202:15318:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:individuals who have searched or would at least be willing to accept a new job if offered, the
8:203:15412:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:survey asks a series of questions relating to their job search (if any), including the reasons for
8:204:15510:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:their decision to (not) search.  It then asks an exhaustive set of questions on the types of effort
8:205:15609:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:exerted when seeking new work (e.g., updating resumes, searching online, contacting employers
8:206:15702:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:directly). It also asks about the number of job applications completed within the last four weeks
8:207:15799:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and the number of employer contacts and job offers received.  It also probes further to see how
8:208:15894:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:those contacts and offers came about, i.e., whether they were the result of traditional search
8:209:15988:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:methods or whether they came about through a referral or an unsolicited employer contact.
8:210:16077:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:For those who received an offer, including any offers within the last six months, the survey asks
8:211:16174:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about a range of characteristics of the job offer, including the wage offered, the expected hours,
8:212:16272:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:its benefits, as well as the type of work to be done and the characteristics of the employer.  It
8:213:16369:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:also asks what led, or may lead, the respondent to accept or reject the offer, and asks a range of
8:214:16467:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:questions about whether there was any bargaining with either the current or future employer.
8:215:16559:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Since only a fraction of respondents in our sample report a job offer in the months leading up
8:216:16653:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to the survey,  we ask those who are currently employed a range of additional,  retrospective
8:217:16746:25:25:81:386:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:questions about the search process that led to their current job.
8:218:16811:25:25:98:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Many  of  the  survey  questions  follow  a  format  similar  to  the  Current  Population  Survey
8:219:16909:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(CPS),  though  there  are  notable  differences.   The  survey  identifies  the  labor  force  status  of
8:220:17015:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:respondents at several different points in their employment history:  at the time of the survey,
8:221:17111:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:at the time of their hiring (if currently employed), and at the time of their job offer (if they
8:222:17207:25:25:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:received an offer within the last six months).  We also impute a labor force status for individuals
8:223:17306:25:0:81:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:four weeks prior to the survey.  Our ability to identify labor force status at these different points
9:224:17408:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:allows us to deal with time aggregation and related issues when comparing the search and job-
9:225:17501:25:25:81:331:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:finding behavior of the employed and non-employed.
9:226:17551:25:25:98:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We define a respondent’s labor force status at the time of the survey in a manner similar
9:227:17640:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to  the  CPS,  but  because  we  ask  about  search  effort  more  broadly  than  the  CPS,  we  can
9:228:17740:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:generate  two  measures  of  unemployment.   The  Bureau  of  Labor  Statistics  (BLS)  definition
9:229:17838:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:classifies someone as unemployed if they “do not have a job, have actively looked for work in
9:230:17931:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  prior  four  weeks,  and  are  currently  available  for  work.”   Those  on  temporary  layoff  are
9:231:18036:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:also  included  regardless  of  search  effort  or  availability.   We  employ  the  same  definition,  but
9:232:18143:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:due to the skip logic of the CPS survey design, there are some non-employed in the CPS who
9:233:18233:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are never asked whether they searched for work.  These are primarily retired individuals who
9:234:18325:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:state that they do not want a job (and are therefore assumed to be unavailable for work).  Our
9:235:18419:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:survey, however, captures search effort regardless of whether a non-employed individual states
9:236:18513:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that they want work.  We define a respondent’s labor force status at the time of the survey
9:237:18604:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:using  the  broader  “job  search”  definition  of  unemployment  since  we  aim  to  capture  overall
9:238:18706:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search activity and its effectiveness within the aggregate economy.  The difference is that the
9:239:18801:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:“job search” definition includes non-employed individuals who did not state that they want to
9:240:18894:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:work but actively searched and are available, while the stricter “BLS definition” includes only
9:241:18989:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:those who additionally state that they want work.  Given the well-known observation that even
9:242:19082:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:workers who state that they are not available for work and they have not searched for work
9:243:19172:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:transition from nonparticipation to employment in the CPS, we view our broader “job search”
9:244:19263:25:25:81:315:210:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:definition of unemployment as a reasonable one.5
9:245:19311:25:24:98:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We identify individuals as either employed or non-employed at the time of their hiring or
9:246:19400:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:receipt of a job offer.  The survey allows for some greater disaggregation of these labor force sta-
9:247:19500:25:16:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tuses and we obtain results similar to those in our main analyses when using the more detailed
9:248:19594:16:16:94:530:120:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:5While our baseline is the job search definition of unemployment, in Appendix A, we replicate our estimates
9:249:19701:16:12:81:529:104:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:on search effort and outcomes using the definition consistent with the CPS survey design.  The results in the
9:250:19810:12:12:81:529:92:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:appendix show that those included in the broader “job search” definition represent about 12 percent of those
9:251:19918:12:0:81:318:80:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:considered out of the labor force under the BLS definition.

10:279:20797:12:12:81:527:289:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:ages 18 to 64.  Job search definition of unemployment includes all non-employed who actively searched and are

10:281:21010:33:25:81:530:244:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:definitions.6We also impute a labor force status four weeks prior to the survey for individuals
10:282:21105:25:25:81:529:219:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:using a range of their responses on employment status, job tenure, non-employment duration,
10:283:21196:25:25:81:529:194:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job  offer  incidence  and  timing,  etc.   We  detail  our  imputation  methodology  in  Appendix  B.
10:284:21298:25:16:81:529:169:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Having a labor force status for individuals one month prior to the survey is useful for when
10:285:21390:16:16:94:530:153:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:6Specifically, the labor force status at the time of hiring distinguishes between those who quit from a previous
10:286:21502:16:12:81:529:137:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:job and those who lost their job immediately prior to starting the current job.  The majority of the employed
10:287:21611:12:12:81:529:125:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:quit from their current job, so the results for this group are very similar to those reported in our analysis.  The
10:288:21726:12:12:81:529:113:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:labor force status at the time of job offer distinguishes between those who were employed either full-time or
10:289:21835:12:12:81:529:101:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:part-time at the time of the offer.  Most individuals were employed full-time, and consequently their results are
10:290:21948:12:12:81:529:89:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:similar to what we report in our analysis.  The vast majority of the non-employed under both definitions report
10:291:22059:12:0:81:155:77:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:actively searching.
11:292:22079:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we apply our empirical findings to the model because the model characterizes a job seeker’s
11:293:22170:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search behavior using their labor force status prior to exerting search effort or receiving any
11:294:22265:25:25:81:132:659:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:job offers.7
11:295:22277:25:25:98:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Our analysis uses a sample from the SCE of individuals aged 18 to 64 pooled across the 2013-
11:296:22369:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:15 surveys.  This provides just under 2,900 observations.  Individuals are only in the SCE for, at
11:297:22467:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:most, one year, so our sample is a panel of repeated cross sections rather than longitudinal.  By
11:298:22564:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:design, the SCE only includes heads of household.  The survey does not ask the self-employed
11:299:22656:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about job search, so the self-employed are generally excluded by construction throughout our
11:300:22748:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job search analysis.  Table 1 presents basic summary statistics for our analysis sample and a
11:301:22841:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:comparable sample using the same months of data from the CPS. The demographic statistics
11:302:22929:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:across the two surveys are roughly similar, though the shares that are married and white are
11:303:23021:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:both higher in the SCE sample.  The employment-to-population ratio, which is unaffected by
11:304:23111:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  differing  unemployment  definitions,  is  somewhat  higher  in  the  SCE  as  well.   Under  the
11:305:23213:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:BLS definition, the unemployment rate in the SCE is slightly higher than the CPS rate, but
11:306:23303:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:not statistically different.  Including the additional job seekers in the “job search” definition
11:307:23400:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:increases the unemployment rate considerably, from 5.3 percent to 8.0 percent, suggesting that
11:308:23494:25:25:81:467:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the BLS definition of unemployment misses some search activity in the economy.
11:309:23572:25:25:98:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:In addition to our main sample,  we also focus on two subsamples of the data.  The first
11:310:23660:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is  the  subsample  of  the  currently  employed  (excluding  the  currently  self-employed).   After
11:311:23761:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:removing respondents with missing data, this sample includes 1,763 respondents.  We use this
11:312:23853:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:subsample to examine the job search behavior that led to their hiring at their current jobs.  The
11:313:23950:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:second is a subsample of all individuals who received a job offer within the last six months.  By
11:314:24047:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:construction, some of these offers will reflect the respondent’s current job, which we identify
11:315:24142:25:16:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:through a separate question in the survey.  After removing offers with only partial data, the
11:316:24235:16:16:94:530:120:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:7We evaluate the performance of our measure of labor force status along several dimensions in the appendix.
11:317:24342:16:12:81:529:104:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:We also merged the SCE labor market module to the SCE monthly survey and used the labor market status
11:318:24443:12:12:81:529:92:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:from the most recent survey available for a given individual, in either September or August of the same year.
11:319:24552:12:0:81:529:80:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:The results using prior labor force status from the SCE monthly are very similar, see Table B2 in the appendix.
12:320:24665:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:sample has 654 observations.  We use this sample to examine a range of job offer characteristics,
12:321:24762:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:including the offer wage distribution, as well as the characteristics of accepted job offers.  Note
12:322:24861:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that we first asked respondents whether they received any offer in the last month, and only if
12:323:24955:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:not, did we ask about offers received in the last 6 months.  Thus the data allow us to determine
12:324:25051:25:36:81:189:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the monthly offer rate.
12:325:25074:36:36:81:169:573:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:3    Evidence
12:326:25087:36:25:81:529:537:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We now turn to our empirical analysis on job search behavior.  We can summarize our main
12:327:25175:25:24:81:529:512:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:findings as follows:  (i) on-the-job search is pervasive among the employed; (ii) those searching
12:328:25272:24:25:81:529:488:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on-the-job receive at least as many offers as the unemployed; (iii) employed job seekers search
12:329:25367:25:25:81:529:463:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:less than the unemployed, but their search is more effective per unit of effort; (iv) the employed
12:330:25465:25:25:81:529:438:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:who do not search still receive a non-negligible number of (mostly unsolicited) offers; (v) the
12:331:25560:25:30:81:425:413:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed receive better-quality offers but are less likely to accept them.
12:332:25634:30:32:81:388:383:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:3.1    Extensive and Intensive Margins of Job Search
12:333:25686:32:25:81:529:351:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We begin with evidence on the basic characteristics of individual job search effort.  It is useful
12:334:25784:25:25:81:529:326:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to analyze the extensive and intensive margins of job search separately since the distribution
12:335:25878:25:25:81:529:301:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  total  search  effort  along  both  dimensions  is  informative  for  thinking  about  the  efficiency
12:336:25984:25:25:81:530:276:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  job  search.8Table  2  reports  the  incidence  of  job  search  by  labor  force  status,  which  we
12:337:26089:25:25:81:529:251:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:interpret as theextensive  marginof job search.  By definition, all unemployed, save for those
12:338:26183:25:25:81:529:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on temporary layoff, search.  Since we employ a search-based definition of unemployment, only
12:339:26276:25:25:81:529:201:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a minimal amount of those out of the labor force engaged in search.9Among the employed,
12:340:26363:25:25:81:529:176:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:roughly 20 percent can be classified as searchers regardless of the criteria we employ to define
12:341:26459:25:25:81:529:151:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job search.  Over 23 percent of employed looked for new work in the last four weeks, with 20
12:342:26551:25:15:81:529:126:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent applying to at least one job and a similar amount searching at least once in the last
12:343:26644:15:16:94:530:111:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:8We borrow this distinction from the well-established literature on labor supply.  Mukoyama, Patterson, and
12:344:26751:16:7:81:399:95:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:S ̧ahin (2017) also apply this distinction to the search effort of the unemployed.
12:345:26833:7:16:94:530:88:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:9By both the “job search” definition and the BLS definition of unemployment, no one outside of the labor
12:346:26937:16:0:81:187:72:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:force is available for work.

13:373:28252:24:25:98:529:214:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Since empirical evidence on the incidence of on-the-job search is scarce and mostly comes
13:374:28341:25:25:81:529:189:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:from outdated surveys, it is hard to provide a good comparison of the pervasiveness of on-the-
13:375:28435:25:25:81:529:164:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job search with other studies.  Time use surveys that rely on diary data on time use, such as
13:376:28528:25:25:81:529:139:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the American Time Use Survey (ATUS), are likely to underestimate search activity since they
13:377:28619:25:16:81:529:114:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:are based on time use the day prior to the survey as we discuss in detail in Appendix A.2.10
13:378:28711:16:16:90:530:98:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:10Table  A4  in  the  appendix  provides  a  comprehensive  comparison  of  the  SCE  and  ATUS  measures  of  job
13:379:28825:16:0:81:141:82:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:search activity.
14:380:28843:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The  ATUS  reports  that,  on  average,  only  around  0.6  percent  of  employed  actively  searched
14:381:28944:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in  the  2013-2015  period  and  the  corresponding  fraction  is  16.5  percent  for  the  unemployed
14:382:29046:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:revealing  the  difficulty  of  comparing  daily  diary-based  measures  with  traditional  surveys.11
14:383:29148:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Some older studies that relied on survey data found pervasive job search activity among the
14:384:29239:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed.  For example, according to Black (1980), around 14 percent of white workers and 10
14:385:29331:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent of black workers reported on-the-job search in the 1972 interview of the Panel Study
14:386:29423:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of Income Dynamics (PSID). Similarly, Blau and Robins (1990) report that employed search
14:387:29511:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:spells  represent  about  10  percent  of  all  employment  spells  in  the  Employment  Opportunity
14:388:29611:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Pilot Projects (EOPP) in 1980.  Unfortunately, the main source of labor market statistics for
14:389:29704:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the U.S., the CPS, does not ask questions about job search to employed individuals, but its
14:390:29795:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:recent Computer and Internet Use Supplements asked all respondents, regardless of their labor
14:391:29888:24:25:81:530:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:force status, whether they used the internet to search for a job in the pastsix months.  Around
14:392:29983:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:28 percent of the employed reported using the internet for job search in the last six months in
14:393:30078:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the 2015 survey.  We also asked a question about whether an individual searched in the last
14:394:30169:25:25:81:530:360:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:twelve months.  Around 45 percent employed reported searching in the last twelve months using
14:395:30262:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:any active search method, including online job search.  Given that we designed our survey to
14:396:30354:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:cast a wide net to identify any “search activity,” we find our estimates for the extensive margin
14:397:30451:25:25:81:179:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of search reasonable.
14:398:30472:25:25:98:530:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f6:A:Table 3 reports the amount of search effort spent on the job search process, theintensive
14:399:30561:25:25:81:530:235:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:marginof job search.  We categorize the employed by whether or not they are actively looking
14:400:30653:25:25:81:530:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for  work.12This  distinction  emphasizes  the  stark  differences  in  search  activity  among  the
14:401:30753:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed.  We find that the unemployed send substantially more job applications and dedicate
14:402:30845:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:more  hours  to  search  than  the  other  groups.   They  put  in  roughly  twice  as  much  effort  as
14:403:30949:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  employed  that  actively  look  for  work.   Figure  1  shows  the  distributions  of  search  time
14:404:31053:25:16:81:530:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:within the lastseven  daysand the number of applications sent within the lastfour  weeksfor
14:405:31144:16:11:90:360:95:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:?:11See also Mueller (2010) for similar evidence for an earlier period.
14:406:31213:11:0:90:263:84:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:?:12The estimates exclude the self-employed.
15:407:31257:0:25:81:530:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed and unemployed job seekers conditional on searching in the last four weeks.13Around
15:408:31349:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:half of the employed and about one-third of the unemployed apply to either one or two jobs.
15:409:31440:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:About 10 percent of employed job seekers sent more than 10 applications, while just over 25
15:410:31531:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent of the unemployed sent more than 10 applications.  The right panel of the figure shows
15:411:31625:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  distribution  of  search  time.   The  differences  between  the  employed  and  unemployed  are
15:412:31726:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:more pronounced when we consider the distribution of search time.  Around 40 percent of the
15:413:31817:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed report searching for one hour or less within the last seven days, but around 80 percent
15:414:31913:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the unemployed searched for longer than one hour.  Moreover, searching for longer than 10
15:415:32005:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:hours a week is relatively more common among the unemployed than the employed (around 35
15:416:32093:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent vs.  15 percent).  Interestingly, even among those people who reported searching in the
15:417:32188:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:last four weeks, 25 percent of the employed and 15 percent of the unemployed did not search
15:418:32279:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:at all within the last seven days.  This observation highlights the intermittent nature of search
15:419:32376:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effort and reinforces our view that ATUS, which is based on a time dairy reported at the daily
15:420:32470:25:25:81:530:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:frequency, greatly understates the extensive margin of job search.14Given that the employed
15:421:32561:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are likely to search during work hours (which they would report in the ATUS as their main
15:422:32650:25:31:81:489:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:activity during that time), the bias is likely to be more pronounced for the employed.
15:423:32736:31:32:81:349:304:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:3.2    Search Outcomes by Labor Force Status
15:424:32780:32:25:81:529:272:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We have shown that there is considerable job search activity among the employed. We now move
15:425:32872:25:24:81:529:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on to show how search effort translates into employer contacts, job offers, and new job matches
15:426:32967:24:25:81:529:223:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for the employed and unemployed.  The fact that our data contain exhaustive information on
15:427:33057:25:25:81:529:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:both search effort and search outcomes at different stages of the process puts us in a unique
15:428:33150:25:16:81:481:173:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:position to assess the relative effectiveness of employed versus unemployed search.15
15:429:33235:16:16:90:530:157:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:13Recall from Table 2 that around 23 percent of the employed report actively searching.  The remainder is
15:430:33340:16:8:81:414:141:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:excluded from the analysis to provide a more relevant comparison of distributions.
15:431:33422:8:11:90:386:133:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:?:14In Appendix Figure C.2, we report these distributions over a finer grid.
15:432:33496:11:16:90:530:122:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:15This is a long-standing question in the labor-search literature and has important implications for on-the-job
15:433:33607:16:12:81:529:106:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:search models as we discuss in Section 4.  Earlier empirical contributions include Holzer (1987) and Blau and
15:434:33716:12:0:81:140:94:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:Robins (1990).

18:541:38257:12:12:81:484:485:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:the hours of time spent searching for work in the last seven days (right panel).  Estimates are for all

18:543:38463:12:33:81:181:461:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:supplements of the SCE.
18:544:38486:33:25:81:529:428:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status at the time of the outcome.  Non-random job acceptances by those unemployed at the
18:545:38575:25:24:81:529:403:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:time of a job offer can create a selection issue.16Since the focus is job search behavior by labor
18:546:38673:24:25:81:529:379:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:force status, we address this issue by constructing a measure of labor force status for the prior
18:547:38770:25:25:81:530:354:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:month using a wide range of survey questions from the SCE labor supplement.17In the middle
18:548:38860:25:25:81:529:329:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:panel of Table 4, we report offer outcomes by prior labor force status.  The results show that the
18:549:38958:25:25:81:529:304:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:fraction with at least one offer over the last four weeks decreases slightly for the employed, from
18:550:39057:25:25:81:529:279:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:11.8 percent to 10.5 percent, but increases substantially for the unemployed, from 22.0 percent
18:551:39152:25:25:81:529:254:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to  33.9  percent,  when  considering  labor  force  status  in  “prior  month”  instead  of  “at  time
18:552:39255:25:25:81:529:229:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  survey”.   This  is  in  line  with  the  expectation  that  some  individuals  who  are  unemployed
18:553:39359:25:25:81:529:204:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:at  the  time  of  the  job  offer  started  working  by  the  time  of  the  survey.   Another  concern  is
18:554:39467:25:25:81:529:179:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that a substantial fraction of employed workers are only seeking an additional job.  Job-to-job
18:555:39562:25:16:81:529:154:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:transitions, as measured in the CPS and most other household surveys, only capture changes
18:556:39652:16:15:90:530:138:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:16This selection issue is similar to the time-aggregation issue that plagues calculations of the separation rate
18:557:39764:15:8:81:147:123:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:using CPS data.
18:558:39779:8:16:90:530:115:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:17In Appendix B, we detail our methodology for determining labor force status in the prior month and evaluate
18:559:39888:16:12:81:529:99:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:our measure along several dimensions.  Unfortunately, the survey does not allow us to directly identify whether a
18:560:40001:12:12:81:529:87:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:respondent was searching on-the-job in the prior month.  We also consider an alternative measure of prior status
18:561:40113:12:0:81:396:75:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:based on the monthly SCE data and show that we obtain vary similar results.
19:562:40190:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in an individual’s main job.  Nearly all models of labor market search only consider this type of
19:563:40287:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job-to-job transition as well.  In the bottom panel of Table 4, we report offer outcomes ignoring
19:564:40384:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the offers of those who reported only looking for additional work.  The fraction of the employed
19:565:40480:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:receiving at least one offer falls to 9.1 percent in this case.  We use this estimate of the offer
19:566:40578:25:25:81:253:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rate in our model calibration below.
19:567:40614:25:25:98:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:It  is  possible  that  some  individuals  simply  do  not  pursue  offers  that  they  are  likely  to
19:568:40717:25:25:81:530:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:reject.  In this case, the job offers we observe in the data would becensored.  Most importantly,
19:569:40814:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this type of censoring could be correlated with employment status.  To address this issue, our
19:570:40908:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:survey asks respondents whether a potential employer indicated that they would be willing to
19:571:41000:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:make an offer but the respondent indicated that he or she was not interested.  We label these
19:572:41093:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offers asunrealizedrejected offers as respondents rejected these offers even before a formal offer
19:573:41191:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:was made.  We indeed find that these unrealized offers are more common for the employed.
19:574:41279:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Among those who did not report a formal offer over the last four weeks, about 4 percent of the
19:575:41373:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed indicated that they rejected such an unrealized offer, compared to only 1 percent of
19:576:41466:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the unemployed.  The bottom panel of Table 4 reports the fraction of individuals who received
19:577:41559:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:at  least  one  offer,  including  these  unrealized  offers.   Accounting  for  unrealized  offers  raises
19:578:41666:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the fraction receiving a job offer to 13.2 percent for the employed and to 34.9 percent for the
19:579:41761:25:25:81:141:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed.
19:580:41772:25:25:98:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Table 5 reports the acceptance rate for offers received within the last four weeks by labor
19:581:41863:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:force status in the prior month.  The results show that the unemployed are much more likely
19:582:41954:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to accept a given offer, with 48 to 53 percent of their offers accepted, depending on whether
19:583:42047:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we include all offers or just the best offer in the denominator of the acceptance rate, compared
19:584:42143:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:to the employed, who accept 26 to 32 percent of their offers, depending on the measure used.18
19:585:42237:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:In  our  calibration,  we  focus  on  the  acceptance  rates  of  the  best  offers,  excluding  offers  for
19:586:42345:25:16:81:528:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:additional work, which is 30.0 percent for the employed and 53.2 percent for the unemployed.
19:587:42437:16:16:90:530:95:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:18The survey only asks respondents if they accepted their best offer.  The acceptance rate including all offers
19:588:42548:16:0:81:311:79:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:assumes that none of the remaining offers were accepted.

20:605:43407:32:25:98:529:438:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Finally, we present the distribution of search effort and search outcomes across the different
20:606:43501:25:25:81:529:413:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:labor  force  categories.   Examining  these  distributions  provides  another  way  of  assessing  the
20:607:43604:25:25:81:529:388:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:relative efficiency of employed and unemployed job seekers.  Table 6 reports the distribution of
20:608:43700:25:25:81:529:363:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:respondents, job applications, and job search outcomes by labor force status.  The unemployed
20:609:43793:25:25:81:529:338:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:make up just over 7 percent of our sample, but account for nearly 40 percent of all job applica-
20:610:43889:25:25:81:529:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:tions sent.  At the same time, they only receive 16 percent of all offers made.  In stark contrast,
20:611:43988:25:25:81:529:288:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed who report not looking for work send no applications by construction but account
20:612:44081:25:25:81:529:263:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for around 28 percent of all employer contacts and receive over 27 percent of all job offers.  This
20:613:44180:25:24:81:529:238:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is due,  in part,  to the fact that they also account for 44 percent of all unsolicited employer
20:614:44276:24:25:81:529:214:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:contacts and 53 percent of all unsolicited offers.  Those actively searching on the job account
20:615:44371:25:25:81:529:189:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for another 48 percent of all job offers.  Thus, the job search behavior of the unemployed can
20:616:44465:25:25:81:529:164:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:be characterized by high effort, but relatively low returns in terms of employer contacts and
20:617:44558:25:25:81:529:139:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job offers.  The employed,  on the other hand,  fare fairly well regardless of whether they are
20:618:44653:25:25:81:529:114:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:actually  looking  for  work.   Though  the  unemployed  are  seemingly  less  effective  in  their  job
20:619:44757:25:0:81:464:89:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search efforts, they are also more likely to accept the offers that they do receive.

21:636:45613:32:25:81:529:412:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The employed are more effective at generating job offers,  but our evidence thus far is silent
21:637:45707:25:25:81:530:387:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on whether the employed receivebetteroffers than the unemployed do.  We now examine how
21:638:45794:25:25:81:529:362:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the job offers themselves, including all offers and the subset of those that are accepted, differ
21:639:45891:25:25:81:529:337:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:by employment status.  Our survey asks individuals about any offers they received in the last
21:640:45984:25:25:81:529:312:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:four weeks.  For those who received no offer within the last four weeks,  it probes further to
21:641:46078:25:24:81:529:287:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:elicit  information  on  any  offers  received  within  the  last  six  months.   The  survey  also  elicits
21:642:46186:24:25:81:529:263:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the respondent’s labor force status at the time of the job offer.  It asks a variety of questions
21:643:46283:25:25:81:529:238:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:about the characteristics of the job offer, including information about the search and bargaining
21:644:46380:25:25:81:484:213:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:process.  It also asks if the offer was accepted (and if it represents their current job).

22:688:47662:33:25:81:529:468:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offer.  The results consistently show that the employed receive much better job offers than the

22:693:48125:25:25:81:529:344:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that, even after accounting for these controls, the distribution of wage offers for the employed
22:694:48221:25:25:81:452:319:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:stochastically dominates the distribution of wage offers for the non-employed.
22:695:48299:25:25:98:529:294:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The remainder of Table 7 shows that job offers received by the employed are superior on
22:696:48386:25:25:81:529:269:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:other  margins  as  well.   Their  hours  are  only  8  log  points  higher  (which  is  not  statistically
22:697:48493:25:25:81:529:244:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:significant),  but  they  are  20  percentage  points  more  likely  to  include  at  least  some  benefits
22:698:48600:25:25:81:529:219:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:such as retirement pay or health insurance.  The employed are nearly twice as likely to have
22:699:48692:25:16:81:529:194:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:received their offer through an unsolicited contact. The employed and non-employed are roughly
22:700:48786:16:15:90:530:178:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:20The offer wage, as well as all other wages in our analysis, refers to the real hourly wage.  Respondents report
22:701:48899:15:12:81:529:163:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:their nominal earnings as an hourly wage, or as a measure of weekly or annual earnings.  In the latter cases, we
22:702:49011:12:13:81:529:151:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:measure the wage as earnings per hour, based on the reported usual hours worked.  We convert all wages used
22:703:49118:13:7:81:302:138:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:into real terms using the Consumer Price Index (CPI).
22:704:49171:7:16:90:530:131:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:21Our conditional estimates of the offered wage and the subsequent accepted wage control for worker and job
22:705:49278:16:12:81:529:115:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:characteristics, as well as state and year fixed effects.  Our worker controls include sex, age, age squared, marital
22:706:49395:12:12:81:530:103:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:status, marital status×sex, education, race, homeowner status, and number of household children.  Our firm and
22:707:49505:12:12:81:529:91:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:job controls are the two-digit occupation of the job and the size of the offering firm.  We report estimates of the
22:708:49620:12:0:81:438:79:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:other job offer characteristics that control for observable characteristics in the appendix.
23:709:49714:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:equally  likely  to  have  had  a  “good  idea”  of  what  the  job  paid  prior  to  receiving  the  offer.
23:710:49822:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Potentially contributing to the differences in offer wages between the two groups, the employed
23:711:49917:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are significantly more likely to bargain over their offers, with 39 percent of their offers involving
23:712:50018:25:25:81:530:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:some bargaining, compared to 24 percent for the non-employed.22Counter-offers by the current
23:713:50110:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employer,  defined  as  anything  from  matching  the  outside  offer  to  offering  a  promotion,  pay
23:714:50213:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:raise, or some added job benefit, occurred for about 14 percent of the employed who received
23:715:50305:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:an offer from an outside firm.  Despite these relatively poor job offers, we still find that the non-
23:716:50406:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed are about one-and-a-half times more likely to accept them, with 55 percent of offers
23:717:50499:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:accepted by the non-employed versus 35 percent by the employed.  The acceptance rates are
23:718:50588:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:very close to those we obtain using the prior month’s labor force status.  Table 7 also suggests
23:719:50684:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that a primary reason that the non-employed are more likely to accept their relatively poorer
23:720:50777:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job offers is a perceived lack of alternative options.  About 27 percent of the non-employed cite
23:721:50874:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a lack of other alternatives as the main reason for accepting an offer, while only 7 percent of
23:722:50969:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed cite that as their primary reason.  The right panel of Figure 2 shows that, even
23:723:51062:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:after controlling for observed worker and job characteristics, the accepted wage distribution of
23:724:51158:25:25:81:518:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed stochastically dominates the accepted wage distribution of the non-employed.
23:725:51247:25:25:98:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We  can  also  examine  job  search  retrospectively  for  those  employed  at  the  time  of  the
23:726:51345:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:survey interview by asking them how they came about their current jobs.  The advantage of
23:727:51434:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this approach is that we are able to examine their starting wages and previous earnings as a
23:728:51526:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:function of their labor force status.  This provides us with additional guidance for our model
23:729:51620:25:25:81:140:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in Section 4.
23:730:51633:25:24:98:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Table 8 presents the characteristics of the current and previous job by labor force status at
23:731:51726:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the time of hire.  We focus on the comparison of the non-employed to those who move directly
23:732:51818:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:from employment to their current job.  At the time of the survey interview, those hired from
23:733:51910:25:16:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:non-employment are paid lower wages, have fewer work hours, and are much less likely to have
23:734:52002:16:16:90:530:95:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:22These estimates are consistent with Hall and Krueger (2012) who find that around a third of all workers
23:735:52107:16:0:81:369:79:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:engaged in some bargaining over their pay with their current employer.

26:804:55552:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:any benefits than those hired while already employed.  They are also somewhat more likely to
26:805:55644:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:be looking for new work at the time of the survey.  Estimates reported in the middle of Table 8
26:806:55739:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:show that most of the wage differences between those hired from employment and those hired
26:807:55829:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:from  non-employment  stem  from  wage  differences  at  their  time  of  hiring.   The  real  starting
26:808:55932:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage of those hired from non-employment is 28 log points lower than the real starting wage
26:809:56022:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of those hired from employment, on average.  Conditioning on the observable characteristics of
26:810:56116:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:the worker and the job reduces the wage difference by nearly half, to about 14 log points.23
26:811:56208:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Despite the large differences in the wage and hours of the current job across the two labor force
26:812:56305:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:categories, the differences in their previous jobs’ wages are small and statistically insignificant.
26:813:56405:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:This  is  true  for  both the  unconditional  real wage and  the  wage  that  controls  for observable
26:814:56507:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:worker  and  job  characteristics.   Note  that  the  smaller  difference  in  the  premium  in  starting
26:815:56612:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wages compared to the difference in offered wages is likely due to a selection issue:  poor job
26:816:56707:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offers are less present in the cross-section of current jobs, as individuals accepting these jobs
26:817:56804:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are more likely to quit and to move to better-paying jobs.  If the non-employed get worse offers
26:818:56900:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:than the employed, this explains why the E-NE gap is smaller among starting wages compared
26:819:56990:25:36:81:150:335:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:to job offers.24
26:820:57006:36:36:81:519:299:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:4    Job Search On and Off the Job:  A Theoretical Framework
26:821:57066:36:25:81:529:263:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:In this section, we set up and calibrate a partial equilibrium model of on-the-job search where,
26:822:57162:25:25:81:529:238:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in the spirit of Christensen et al.  (2005), both employed and unemployed workers face a random
26:823:57257:25:25:81:529:213:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:arrival of job offers.  We do not distinguish between unemployment and nonemployment in our
26:824:57348:25:16:81:529:188:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model since our survey responses show that most of those who were non-employed when they
26:825:57436:16:15:90:530:172:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:23Our conditional estimates of the starting wage and previous wage use the same worker characteristics as our
26:826:57545:15:13:81:529:157:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:conditional estimates of the offer wage.  The starting wage uses the same firm and job controls, and additionally
26:827:57658:13:12:81:529:144:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:controls for two-digit industry.  The previous wage only includes the two-digit occupation as a job or firm control.
26:828:57774:12:12:81:529:132:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:We report estimates of the other characteristics of the current job that control for observable characteristics in
26:829:57888:12:7:81:136:120:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:the appendix.
26:830:57901:7:16:90:530:113:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:24Figure C2 in the appendix shows the distribution of starting wages relative to workers’ prior wages with
26:831:58007:16:12:81:529:97:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:and without controlling for observables.  Even after conditioning out our controls, those who transition directly
26:832:58120:12:12:81:529:85:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:from employment receive a 8 log point increase in their wage, on average, while those who were non-employed
26:833:58227:12:0:81:310:73:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:receive a 13 log point decrease in their wage, on average.
27:834:58287:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:received a job offer were actively searching for work.  We consider four model variations within
27:835:58383:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this framework, ranging from a baseline model where we assume that search effort is constant
27:836:58475:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and  exogenous  to  a  model  where  search  is  endogenous  and  both  search  efficiency  and  the
27:837:58575:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage offer distribution vary by employment status.  We then evaluate how well the search and
27:838:58667:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:acceptance decisions in each version of the model match those observed in our data and relate to
27:839:58763:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the discussion in Hornstein, Krusell, and Violante (2011), who argued that plausibly-calibrated
27:840:58858:25:25:81:445:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search models typically imply implausibly low flow values of unemployment.
27:841:58932:25:25:98:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:One can think of a variety of reasons why wage offers are better, on average, for the employed
27:842:59026:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:compared to the unemployed as we discuss following our model calibration results.  It is also
27:843:59119:25:25:81:530:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:possible that these differences arise simply because we cannotfullycontrol for the differences
27:844:59213:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in  the  characteristics  of  the  employed  and  the  non-employed  workers,  often  referred  to  as
27:845:59315:24:25:81:530:435:11:g_d0_f6:11:g_d0_f4:B:unobserved heterogeneity.  Those with higher unobserved skills are more likely to be employed
27:846:59408:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and more likely to earn a higher wage.  This creates a selection issue that naturally generates a
27:847:59505:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage gap between the job offers of the employed and unemployed.  We incorporate unobserved
27:848:59595:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:heterogeneity within the offer wage distribution into our model and exploit additional moments
27:849:59689:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:from our survey to quantify its importance in accounting for the wage offer differential between
27:850:59785:25:31:81:257:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed and the non-employed.
27:851:59819:31:32:81:177:279:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:4.1    Framework
27:852:59835:32:24:81:529:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The model economy is comprised of homogeneous, risk neutral workers who can search on the
27:853:59924:24:25:81:530:223:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job or while unemployed.25We allow for differences in search efficiency between the employed
27:854:60016:25:25:81:530:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f17:B:and unemployed.  Wage offers,w, are drawn from an i.i.d.  distribution with upper support   ̄w
27:855:60110:25:25:81:530:173:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and c.d.f.F(w), i.e.,F(w) equals the probability a wagew′≤wis drawn.  We allow for the
27:856:60196:25:16:81:529:148:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:possibility that the wage offer distribution varies for the employed and unemployed,  so that
27:857:60289:16:16:90:530:132:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:25Our model is closely related to the extension of Christensen et al.  (2005) derived by Hornstein, Krusell, and
27:858:60401:16:12:81:530:116:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:Violante (2011), but it maintains the distinction between search effort,si, and search efficiency,βi, since our
27:859:60512:12:12:81:529:104:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:data can separately identify the two, and allows for censoring in wage offers as well as exogenous differences in
27:860:60625:12:12:81:529:92:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:the wage offer distribution between the employed and unemployed.  Our model is also related to Lise (2013) who
27:861:60735:12:0:81:392:80:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:introduces precautionary savings into a model with endogenous search effort.
28:862:60813:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed draw from a distributionFe(w) and the unemployed draw from a distribution
28:863:60899:25:25:81:113:684:11:g_d0_f17:11:g_d0_f4:B:Fu(w).
28:864:60905:25:25:98:530:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Time is discrete and an individual receives a job offer with probabilityλi(s) =αi+βis,
28:865:60991:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wheres∈[0,1−αiβi] is the endogenously-chosen level of search effort.26The constantαireflects
28:866:61083:25:25:81:530:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the possibility of unsolicited offers, which occur absent of any search effort, andβireflects search
28:867:61183:25:25:81:530:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:efficiency.  The subscripti∈ {e,u}captures differences in unsolicited offer arrival rates and
28:868:61276:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search efficiency by employment status.  These will generally lead to differential job-offer arrival
28:869:61376:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rates as well.  Search effort has an increasing, convex cost that may also vary by employment
28:870:61469:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status,ci(s), withc′i,c′′i>0 andci(0) =c′i(0) = 0.  Existing matches end exogenously at a rate
28:871:61563:25:25:81:530:484:11:g_d0_f17:11:g_d0_f4:B:δ, and the discount rate isr.  Finally, we introduceex anteheterogeneity in worker productivity,
28:872:61659:25:24:81:530:459:11:g_d0_f17:11:g_d0_f4:B:x, into our model.  This reflects the unobserved heterogeneity that we do not capture in our
28:873:61751:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:empirical analysis and may cause some of the observed wage offer gap between the employed
28:874:61840:25:25:81:530:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and unemployed.  Theex  anteheterogeneity also affects a worker’s cost of search effort and

30:965:66180:25:24:81:529:373:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:As documented in the empirical section,  employed workers appear to reject a non-negligible
