30:975:66756:30:32:81:178:147:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:4.2    Calibration
30:976:66774:32:25:81:529:115:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We calibrate four versions of the model to evaluate and better understand the implications of
30:977:66867:25:14:81:529:90:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:our empirical findings for the model’s key properties.  The first version is a standard model of
30:978:66963:14:0:90:411:76:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:?:30See the appendix of Hall and Mueller (2017) who make the same assumption.
31:979:67040:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on-the-job search with exogenous search effort, where we set search effort to unity, ignore the
31:980:67135:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:role of unsolicited job offers, and assume that the employed and unemployed draw from the same
31:981:67229:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage offer distribution.  The second version extends the standard model to include endogenous
31:982:67322:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search effort and unsolicited job offers but maintains the assumption of homogeneous workers
31:983:67414:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and an identical wage offer distribution for the employed and unemployed.  The third version
31:984:67506:25:25:81:530:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the model allows forex anteworker heterogeneity in productivity, and the fourth version of
31:985:67599:25:25:81:530:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the model allows forex  anteheterogeneity in worker productivity as well as differential wage
31:986:67692:25:25:81:359:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offer distributions between the employed and unemployed.
31:987:67748:25:25:98:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We  calibrate  the  model  at  the  monthly  frequency  and  set  the  monthly  discount  rate  to
31:988:67846:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:match an annual interest rate of 4 percent.  We set the average monthly job separation rate to
31:989:67940:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:be 0.015, which matches the average employment-to-unemployment flow rate in the CPS and
31:990:68027:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in the monthly SCE in recent years.  We assume the wage offer distribution to be log normal,
31:991:68119:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:normalize the mean of the log offered wages to zero, and calibrate the standard deviation of the
31:992:68215:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage offer distribution to be 0.24 as in Hall and Mueller (2017).  This estimate is close to other
31:993:68313:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:estimates of frictional wage dispersion, see, e.g., Low, Meghir and Pistaferri (2010) and Tjaden
31:994:68409:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and Wellschmied (2014).  We choose this estimate of wage dispersion over one derived from the
31:995:68502:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:SCE data because of the relatively small sample of wage offers we observe in the SCE data.
31:996:68592:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:In the full model, we set the mean log offered wage of the employed to match the difference in
31:997:68686:25:25:81:520:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:mean wage offers observed in the SCE data after one controls for observable characteristics.
31:998:68778:25:25:98:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We calibrate the remaining parameters to match the moments derived from our survey, as
31:999:68864:25:25:81:530:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:shown in Table 9.  First, we calibrate theβi’s to match the observed job offer arrival rates for
31:1000:68960:25:24:81:530:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:all offers (including unsolicited offers) for the employed and unemployed in the data.31Recall
31:1001:69054:24:25:81:530:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that we assume an offer production function of the formλi(s) =αi+βisand thusβiis a key
31:1002:69140:25:16:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:determinant of the returns to search.  For the model versions with endogenous search effort, we
31:1003:69235:16:16:90:530:120:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:31We set the offer arrival rates equal to the probability of receiving at least one offer over the course of the
31:1004:69347:16:12:81:529:104:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:last four weeks, ignoring offers for additional jobs, i.e., jobs where a worker does not leave her current employer.
31:1005:69463:12:12:81:529:92:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:We measure search effort as the average number of applications sent over the last four weeks,  again ignoring
31:1006:69572:12:0:81:189:80:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:search for additional work.
32:1007:69601:0:0:81:501:707:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:follow Christensen et al.  (2005) and let the search effort cost function beci(s) =κis1+
32:1008:69689:0:7:503:507:716:6:g_d0_f7:6:g_d0_f7:?:1
32:1009:69690:7:27:502:530:709:6:g_d0_f21:11:g_d0_f4:A:γand
32:1010:69694:27:25:81:530:682:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:use their estimate ofγ= 1.19.  Note that, unlike Christensen et al.  (2005), we assume that
32:1011:69785:25:25:81:530:657:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the cost function differs for the employed and unemployed by the scaling parameterκi.  We
32:1012:69874:25:25:81:530:632:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:normalize the search effort of the unemployed to unity and set theκi’s to match the normalized
32:1013:69968:25:25:81:530:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search efforts of the unemployed and employed.  We set theαi’s to match the unsolicited offer
32:1014:70061:25:25:81:529:582:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rates of the employed and unemployed.  As discussed in the empirical section, unrealized offers
32:1015:70156:25:24:81:529:557:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:represent a non-negligible fraction of overall offers among employed workers,  and we set the
32:1016:70249:24:25:81:530:533:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:parametersχito match the unrealized offer rates for both the employed and unemployed.  We
32:1017:70338:25:25:81:530:508:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:calibratebto match the average acceptance rate of the unemployed.  This allows our model
32:1018:70426:25:25:81:529:483:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:calibrations, by assumption, to match their job-finding rate, as both the acceptance rate and
32:1019:70519:25:25:81:529:458:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the offer rate of the unemployed are targets in the calibration.  The key test then is whether
32:1020:70613:25:25:81:454:433:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:the different models can match the average acceptance rate of theemployed.32
32:1021:70689:25:25:98:529:408:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:As Table 9 shows, in the SCE data, the offer rate of the employed is around 9 percent and the
32:1022:70782:25:25:81:529:383:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:acceptance rate is 30 percent, implying a target of 2.7 percent for the job-to-job transition rate.
32:1023:70881:25:25:81:529:358:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:This rate is above the average employer-to-employer transition rate in the CPS, which averages
32:1024:70975:25:25:81:529:333:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:around 1.9 percent for our sample period.33However, there are also job-to-job transitions that
32:1025:71069:25:24:81:529:308:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:do not involve employer changes.  For the time period that we consider, around 1 percent of
32:1026:71160:24:25:81:529:284:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed  workers  reported  that  the  usual  activities  and  duties  of  their  current  job  changed
32:1027:71264:25:25:81:529:259:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:since the prior month and another 0.9 percent reported a change in job description in the CPS.
32:1028:71358:25:25:81:529:234:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Adding up all these transition implies a job-to-job transition rate of 3.8 percent.  Our target
32:1029:71453:25:25:81:529:209:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for  the  job-to-job  transition  rate  in  the  SCE  (2.7  percent)  is  between  the  strict  and  broad
32:1030:71559:25:25:81:529:184:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:definitions of the job-to-job transition rate in the CPS. If we think of the job-ladder model as
32:1031:71655:25:16:81:529:159:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a change in job description and salary, it is natural to take into account internal moves when
32:1032:71749:16:16:90:530:143:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:32Another option would be to assume that it is equal to a specific value as in Shimer (2005) or Hall and Milgrom
32:1033:71861:16:12:81:529:127:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:(2008),  but we prefer our approach of inferring it directly from our data,  because there is little consensus of
32:1034:71974:12:7:81:369:115:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:what the appropriate level ofbis, except that it should not be too low.
32:1035:72045:7:16:90:530:108:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:33The calculation of the transition rate from one employer to the other is based on the following question:
32:1036:72152:16:12:81:529:92:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:Last month, it was reported that you worked for (employer’s name).  Do you still work for (employer’s name)
32:1037:72259:12:0:81:160:80:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:(at your main job)?
33:1038:72280:0:25:81:195:709:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:calibrating the model.34
33:1039:72304:25:25:98:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:For the model withex anteheterogeneity, we assume that there are two types of workers,
33:1040:72390:25:25:81:530:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a low-xand a high-xtype.  We calibrate thexfor each type by setting it toxlow=−σxand
33:1041:72474:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f17:11:g_d0_f4:B:xhigh=−σx, whereσxis chosen to match the standard deviation of residual wage offers that
33:1042:72562:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:control  for  unobserved  worker  and  job  characteristics.   We  choose  to  target  the  residualized
33:1043:72666:25:25:81:447:584:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:moments since our goal is to quantify the role ofunobservedheterogeneity.35
33:1044:72741:25:25:98:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:A key issue in the models with worker-heterogeneity is the skewness of the unemployment
33:1045:72828:25:25:81:530:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:pool towards the low-xtypes.  For the purpose of our calibration here, we assume that (1) the
33:1046:72921:25:25:81:530:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:average ofδ(x) matches a monthly separation rate of 0.015 and (2) we match the difference in
33:1047:73013:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:prior wages between the employed and unemployed that can not be explained by observable
33:1048:73100:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:characteristics.   The  latter  is  the  critical  moment  that  identifies  the  skewness  of  the  unem-
33:1049:73206:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ployment pool towards the low-productivity types.  In other words,  the larger the difference
33:1050:73299:25:25:81:530:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:betweenδ(xlow) andδ(xhigh), the lower the average productivity of the unemployed and thus
33:1051:73388:25:31:81:339:385:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:the lower the average prior wage of the unemployed.36
33:1052:73441:31:32:81:529:354:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:F:4.3    Implications for Wage Dispersion and the Flow Value of Unemployment
33:1053:73515:32:25:81:529:322:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Table 9 shows the simulation results for all four versions of our model. In the basic version of the
33:1054:73615:25:16:81:529:297:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model, which has exogenous offer arrival rates and search effort, the unemployed are 2.7 times
33:1055:73709:16:16:90:530:281:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:34A longstanding literature in personnel economics documented the importance of internal hires.  For example,
33:1056:73818:16:12:81:529:265:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:Lazear and Oyer (2004), using employee-employer data from Sweden show that firms fill a substantial fraction of
33:1057:73929:12:12:81:529:253:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:jobs internally and that the rate of internal hiring relative to external hiring increases monotonically at higher
33:1058:74043:12:12:81:529:241:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:levels.  At lower levels around 40 percent of jobs are filled internally while at the higher levels, almost 90 percent
33:1059:74161:12:7:81:494:229:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:of hires come from within the firm.  This evidence points to the prominence of within-firm job ladders.
33:1060:74264:7:24:90:397:222:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:?:35To be more precise, we assume that our observed offered wage,  ̃y, satisfies
33:1061:74342:24:20:236:530:198:9:g_d0_f20:9:g_d0_f8:F:log( ̃y) =log(y) +log(x) +εy,(7)
33:1062:74374:20:23:81:504:178:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:wherelog(y)∼N(μi,σy),log(x)∼N(0,σx) andεy∼N(0,σεy) are independently distributed, and thus,

33:1064:74470:0:10:273:282:165:9:g_d0_f25:9:g_d0_f25:A:√
33:1065:74471:10:20:282:530:155:9:g_d0_f20:9:g_d0_f8:F:σ2y+σ2x+σ2εy.(8)
33:1066:74487:20:12:81:529:135:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:We assume a moderate degree of measurement error of 13 percent of the unconditional variance in offered wages,
33:1067:74597:12:15:81:529:123:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:consistent with Bound and Krueger (1991).  Given our calibration of bothσ2εyandσ2y, we get an estimate for

35:1117:76532:0:25:81:530:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:more efficient at search than the employed, as implied by the ratioβu/βe.  This is because the

35:1120:76809:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effort,  however,  imply  the  opposite  finding.   In  all  three  cases,  the  models  predict  that  the
35:1121:76916:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed have a search efficiency parameter of 0.93-0.95, while the unemployed have a search
35:1122:77008:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:efficiency parameter of 0.32, implying that the employed are almost three times more efficient
35:1123:77102:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:per unit of search effort.  The high level of search efficiency and low level of search effort among
35:1124:77202:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed implies a very high cost of search for the employed in our calibration.  Censoring
35:1125:77297:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the wage offer distribution due to unrealized, rejected offers appears to be negligible for the
35:1126:77395:25:25:81:530:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed  but  substantial  for  the  employed,  as  indicated  by  the  parametersχi.   About  6
35:1127:77494:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent of offers are unrealized for the unemployed but 35-39 percent of offers are unrealized
35:1128:77588:24:25:81:164:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for the employed.
35:1129:77605:25:25:98:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:In our baseline version of the model, the job offer acceptance rate of the employed is 17.6
35:1130:77696:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent, well below the empirical estimate of 30.0 percent in our survey.  In this version, workers
35:1131:77795:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:who move up the wage ladder reject most offers once they get near the top.  The versions of the
35:1132:77890:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model with endogenous search effort improve on this substantially.  The acceptance rate rises
35:1133:77983:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to over 21 percent in the two intermediate versions of the model where we allow for endogenous
35:1134:78077:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search but maintain an identical wage offer distribution for the employed and unemployed.  In
35:1135:78170:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:these versions, workers at the bottom of the wage ladder are more likely to accept an offer and,
35:1136:78266:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:due to endogenous search, are also more likely to receive an offer than those at the top of the
35:1137:78361:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ladder.  When we include differing wage offer distributions by employment status, we match
35:1138:78451:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the empirical acceptance rate of the employed exactly.  In this version of the model, the wage
35:1139:78545:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offer distribution of the employed stochastically dominates the wage offer distribution of the
35:1140:78639:25:25:81:530:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed, so the acceptance rate for an employed worker at the reservation wageRexceeds
35:1141:78728:25:25:81:300:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the acceptance rate of an unemployed worker.
35:1142:78772:25:0:98:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Our  first  two  versions  of  the  model,  which  ignore  unobserved  heterogeneity,  generate  a
36:1143:78872:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:positive but negligible wage offer gap between the employed and unemployed of just two log
36:1144:78962:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:points.  This is due to the differential censoring of the wage offer distribution by employment
36:1145:79057:25:25:81:113:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status.
36:1146:79064:25:25:98:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:When we add heterogeneity to the model but maintain identical wage offer distributions for
36:1147:79154:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed and the unemployed (i.e., the third version of the model), the wage offer gap only
36:1148:79249:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rises to 4 log points.  Half of the gap is due to censoring while the other half is due to worker
36:1149:79346:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:heterogeneity.  Adding worker heterogeneity, we match the differential in prior wages of about
36:1150:79440:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:-10 log points between the employed and unemployed.  In both the model and the data, prior
36:1151:79530:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wages are higher for the unemployed.  This is because the prior wages of the employed tend to
36:1152:79623:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:be from jobs further down the wage ladder, while the prior wages of the unemployed are wages
36:1153:79715:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:prior to a separation and thus further up on the wage ladder.  With worker-heterogeneity, the
36:1154:79808:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:pool of unemployed is skewed towards the low-xtypes.  This reduces the gap in prior wages
36:1155:79897:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:between the employed and the unemployed so as to match the empirically-observed gap (to see
36:1156:79988:25:25:81:286:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this, compare columns 2 and 3 in Table 9).
36:1157:80030:25:25:98:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:In the full version of the model, which additionally allows for a differing wage offer distri-
36:1158:80124:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:bution by employment status, we impose a 25 log point wage offer gap based on the observed
36:1159:80214:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:gap in the data once we control for observable characteristics.  Interestingly, this version of the
36:1160:80313:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model attributes a larger fraction of the gap to unobserved worker heterogeneity.  The reason
36:1161:80406:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is that,  in this model version,  for a givenx,  there is a larger differential in prior wages.  As
36:1162:80505:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a consequence, the pool of unemployed needs to be more skewed towards the low-xtypes for
36:1163:80593:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the model to match the observed differential in prior wages.  The unemployment rate of the
36:1164:80683:25:24:81:530:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:low-xtype is 8.9 percent as compared to 6.5 percent for the high-xtype,  implying that the
36:1165:80773:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:share of the low-productivity group is 57.7 percent among the unemployed.  This is consistent
36:1166:80866:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with Mueller (2017) who finds in the CPS data that unemployment risk is 36 percent higher
36:1167:80955:25:25:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for workers below the median residual wage (i.e., after controlling for observables) compared
36:1168:81048:25:0:81:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to those above the median residual wage, implying that the share of low-residual wage workers
37:1169:81143:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:among the unemployed is 57.6 percent in the CPS data.  Yet, despite this non-trivial degree
37:1170:81234:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  negative  selection  among  the  unemployed  in  our  model,  it  accounts  for  only  about  7  log
37:1171:81338:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:points out of the 25 log point residual wage premium of the employed.  The contribution of
37:1172:81428:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:censoring  remains  small,  again  explaining  only  two  log  points  of  the  wage  offer  gap.   Thus,
37:1173:81533:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:our  calibration  of  the  full  model  suggests  that  neither  unobserved  worker  heterogeneity  nor
37:1174:81636:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:differential censoring of the wage offer distribution can explain the remaining 16 log points (64
37:1175:81733:25:25:81:227:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:percent) of the wage offer gap.
37:1176:81764:25:25:98:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Table 9 also reports the flow value of unemployment relative to the average wage in the
37:1177:81851:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:economy.  Hornstein, Krusell, and Violante (2011) advocate that any search model that aims
37:1178:81941:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to  fit  transition  rates  and  wage  dispersion  should  have  its  performance  evaluated  using  its
37:1179:82045:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:implied flow value of unemployment.  The reason is that search models often imply very low
37:1180:82135:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or even negative flow values.  Our benchmark job-ladder model without search effort implies
37:1181:82226:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a flow value of unemployment of 0.33, which is slightly below the lower end of the parameter
37:1182:82318:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:values used in the literature.  Note that the dispersion of offer wages used for this exercise is
37:1183:82415:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:relatively modest, as in Hall and Mueller (2017).  If we used a higher level of wage dispersion,
37:1184:82511:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the benchmark model would fare worse, implying an even lower flow value of unemployment.
37:1185:82599:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:The models with endogenous search effort do substantially better at producing a reasonable
37:1186:82689:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:flow  value  of  unemployment  of  between  0.50  and  0.69,  depending  on  whether  one  nets  out
37:1187:82789:25:25:81:140:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search costs.
37:1188:82802:25:25:98:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Finally,  our  model  with  differential  offer  distributions  by  employment  status  performs
37:1189:82898:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:demonstrably better than the other three models in matching the amount of wage dispersion
37:1190:82987:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:observed in the data.  Hornstein,  Krusell,  and Violante (2011) argue that a standard model
37:1191:83079:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of frictional search and matching in the labor market can only account for a tiny fraction of
37:1192:83172:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the wage dispersion observed in the data.  They find that extending the model to include on-
37:1193:83264:25:25:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the-job search can increase the ratio of the mean wage to the minimum wage observed in the
37:1194:83354:25:0:81:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:data (the mean-min ratio), as high as 1.4, but not nearly as high as the 1.7 ratio they observe
38:1195:83451:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in the data.  Our baseline model that includes on-the-job search yields a mean-min wage ratio
38:1196:83544:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of 1.43, very similar to their estimate.  Endogenizing search effort increases the implied ratio
38:1197:83640:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to 1.48.  The model, which also allows for heterogeneity in wage offer distributions, increases
38:1198:83735:25:25:81:530:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the ratio (conditional on worker-heterogeneityx) to 1.68.  The ability of our model to generate
38:1199:83830:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage dispersion that is consistent with the data is particularly notable because it does so while
38:1200:83927:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:yielding a reasonable value for the flow utility of unemployment of 0.74 and matching the ac-
38:1201:84020:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ceptance rate of the employed in our data.  These results are remarkably robust to a number
38:1202:84111:25:30:81:422:534:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:of alternative calibrations, as we discuss in the robustness subsection.37
38:1203:84185:30:32:81:515:504:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:4.4    Implications for Labor Force History and Unobserved Heterogeneity
38:1204:84257:32:25:81:530:472:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Our  model  predicts  a  correlation  betweenex  anteunobserved  heterogeneity  and  labor  force
38:1205:84354:25:25:81:529:447:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:histories that provides some helpful guidance in addressing unobserved heterogeneity in em-
38:1206:84445:25:25:81:530:422:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:pirical work.  Specifically, even controlling for theircurrentlabor force status, low-xworkers
38:1207:84539:25:25:81:529:397:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:were more likely to be unemployed and less likely to have worked in the past.  This observation
38:1208:84634:25:25:81:529:372:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:makes the work history of a worker a useful proxy for unobserved heterogeneity.  Our survey
38:1209:84725:25:25:81:529:347:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:allows us to control for workers’ labor force history over the last five years.  As Table 10 shows,
38:1210:84824:25:25:81:529:322:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:controlling  for  the  fraction  of  the  last  five  years  that  the  worker  was  employed  reduces  the
38:1211:84931:25:25:81:529:297:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage offer gap from 0.25 to 0.22.  When we additionally control for share of time unemployed
38:1212:85023:25:25:81:529:272:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and  share  of  time  spent  as  a  student,  the  difference  goes  down  to  0.21.   When  we  run  the
38:1213:85128:25:24:81:529:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:same regression on the model-generated data, we find that the difference goes down from 0.25
38:1214:85220:24:25:81:529:223:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to  0.21,  very  similar  to  the  data  verifying  the  usefulness  of  labor  force  history  as  a  control
38:1215:85330:25:25:81:529:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for  unobserved  heterogeneity.   The  congruence  of  the  regression  results  in  the  data  and  the
38:1216:85434:25:16:81:529:173:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model is particularly noteworthy because the calibration of our model exploits an independent
38:1217:85527:16:16:90:530:157:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:37It is not clear what the empirical target for the dispersion in offered wages should be.  Hornstein, Krusell, and
38:1218:85642:16:12:81:529:141:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:Violante (2011) find mean-min ratios of 1.48 to above 2, depending on how narrowly they define a given labor
38:1219:85750:12:12:81:529:129:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:market.  Our measure of dispersion is taken from Hall and Mueller (2017) who estimate the standard deviation
38:1220:85858:12:12:81:529:117:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:of wage offers for a given worker at 0.24.  This estimate is at the upper end of other papers who identify the
38:1221:85968:12:12:81:529:105:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:dispersion of wage offers from wage changes associated with job switches for a given individual (e.g.,  Tjaden
38:1222:86078:12:12:81:529:93:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:and Wellschmied, 2014).  Interestingly, the mean-min ratio in our model with endogenous search effort exactly
38:1223:86187:12:0:81:473:81:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:matches the lower end of the mean-min wage ratios reported in Hornstein, Krusell, and Violante.
39:1224:86284:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:source of variation in prior wages rather than employment histories.  It is, of course, possible
39:1225:86380:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to go one step further and ask how powerful a proxy work history is to control for unobserved
39:1226:86473:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:heterogeneity since our model provides us with an estimate of the role of unobserved hetero-
39:1227:86565:25:25:81:530:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:geneity.  If we shut down the differences inx, the wage offer differential goes down from 0.25
39:1228:86659:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to 0.18 suggesting that controlling for work history controls for more than half of the effect of
39:1229:86756:25:25:81:212:584:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:unobserved heterogeneity.38

39:1248:87896:42:32:81:178:258:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:4.5    Robustness
39:1249:87913:32:25:81:529:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We consider several extensions of our baseline calibration in order to assess the robustness of
39:1250:88008:25:25:81:529:201:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:our quantitative findings.  In this section,  we discuss the results of five robustness exercises.
39:1251:88106:25:25:81:529:176:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:In Appendix D, we report the estimates from these exercises using the versions of our model
39:1252:88197:25:24:81:529:151:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(i) with unobserved worker heterogeneity (the equivalent of column 3 in Table 9) and (ii) with
39:1253:88291:24:25:81:530:127:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unobserved worker heterogeneityanddiffering wage offer distributions for the employed and
39:1254:88380:25:17:81:350:102:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the unemployed (the equivalent of column 4 in Table 9).
39:1255:88435:17:0:90:257:85:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:?:38See the appendix for additional details.
40:1256:88479:0:25:81:530:709:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f4:B:Alternative calibration strategy for censoring of offers:One interpretation of our find-
40:1257:88567:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ings is that the employed are more selective in their job search and apply only to jobs that
40:1258:88659:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:they think would dominate their current job.  Furthermore, workers further up the wage ladder
40:1259:88752:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:should  be  more  selective.   We  address  this  concern  in  our  baseline  model  by  accounting  for
40:1260:88856:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:censoring of the wage offer distribution.  The results in Table 9 show that this only accounts
40:1261:88950:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for eight percent of the observed wage offer gap.  Our approach, however, only addresses the
40:1262:89042:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rejection of unrealized offers.  It does not account for censoring due to employers passing on
40:1263:89136:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:extending job offers to those they feel are out of their reach.  We address this possibility by cal-
40:1264:89236:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ibrating a version of the model where the offer distributions are assumed to be the same for the
40:1265:89332:25:25:81:530:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed and unemployed, but choose the censoring parameter,χe, to match the acceptance
40:1266:89419:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rate of the employed, which constitutes a natural upper bound on the extent of censoring in
40:1267:89510:24:25:81:530:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed offers.39This version implies a substantial incidence of censoring of the wage offers
40:1268:89604:25:25:81:530:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the employed, withχeof 0.72, and thus endogenously generates a wage offer differential of
40:1269:89696:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:observed offers of 0.08.  However, this calibration generates only half of the wage offer differ-
40:1270:89793:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ential observed in the data (0.12 in the model vs.  0.25 in the data) suggesting that even if
40:1271:89886:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we attribute the high acceptance rate of the employed to censoring, the model still falls short
40:1272:89981:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of explaining the wage offer differential.  At the same time, it is worth pointing out that this
40:1273:90077:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:version generates a reasonable flow value of unemployment of 0.70 and a mean-min ratio of 1.58
40:1274:90171:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:even though we do not allow for any exogenous wage offer differential.  The reason is that this
40:1275:90266:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:version of the model implies a very high underlying contact rate for the employed, and thus
40:1276:90357:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:limits the option value of unemployment to a similar extent as the model with an exogenous
40:1277:90447:25:24:81:187:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage offer differential.
40:1278:90471:24:25:81:530:161:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f4:B:Variance  of  wage  offers:If  we  calibrate  the  variance  of  wage  offers  to  be  twice  as  high
40:1279:90573:25:17:81:530:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(i.e.,σy= 0.34), the implied flow values for our models are 0.52 and 0.30 in the models with
40:1280:90665:17:16:90:530:119:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:39We also calibrated our models without any censoring of potential offers, which is the typical assumption in
40:1281:90774:16:12:81:529:103:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:calibration of job ladder models.  This version produces somewhat lower wage dispersion and implies a lower
40:1282:90881:12:0:81:370:91:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:flow value of unemployment than the baseline but differences are small.
41:1283:90954:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:heterogeneity with and without differential wage offer distributions, respectively.  The implied
41:1284:91050:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage dispersion as measured by the mean-min ratio is of course larger under this calibration
41:1285:91142:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the model, increasing from 1.48 to 1.75 in the model without differential offer distributions
41:1286:91238:25:25:81:270:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and from 1.68 to 1.94 in the full model.
41:1287:91278:25:25:81:530:609:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f4:B:Alternative calibration strategy for the curvature of the search cost function:We
41:1288:91359:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:also assessed to what extent the versions of our model with endogenous search effort match
41:1289:91449:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the observed distribution of job search effort among the employed.  We focus on search effort
41:1290:91542:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:at  the  bottom  of  the  wage  ladder,  since  it  determines  how  quickly  a  worker  transitions  up
41:1291:91646:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  ladder  should  she  choose  to  accept  a  low-paying  job.   The  versions  of  our  model  with
41:1292:91749:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:worker heterogeneity imply job search effort of 0.26-0.27 (relative to the search effort of the
41:1293:91844:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed)  at  the  95th  percentile  of  the  stationary  effort  distribution,  which  is  somewhat
41:1294:91947:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:below  the  0.38  value  observed  in  the  data.   The  shape  of  the  search  effort  distribution  for
41:1295:92053:25:25:81:530:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed is driven by the parameterγ, which determines the convexity of the search cost
41:1296:92144:25:25:81:530:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:function.  If we setγ= 2.5,  we are able to roughly match the observed search effort at the
41:1297:92235:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:95th percentile of the distribution.  These versions of the model generate more search at the
41:1298:92328:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:bottom of the wage ladder and less at the top, consistent with our empirical findings.  Implied
41:1299:92423:25:25:81:423:310:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f5:B:flow values for unemployment and mean-min ratios are little changed.40
41:1300:92493:25:25:81:530:285:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f4:B:Alternative calibration of the job-finding rate:The offer and acceptance rates in our
41:1301:92578:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:calibration imply a monthly unemployment-to-employment transition rate of 18 percent.  This
41:1302:92669:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is lower than the transition rate used in Hornstein, Krusell, and Violante (2011), which they
41:1303:92762:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:target directly.  This is partly driven by their sample period (1994-2007) and by their use of
41:1304:92856:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:total unemployment outflows (both to employment and out of the labor force) when calculating
41:1305:92948:24:16:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the transition rate.  To see how this affects our calibration,  we increase the offer rate of the
41:1306:93045:16:16:90:530:145:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:F:40Another robustness check we performed was to target the acceptance rate of all offers as opposed to the
41:1307:93150:16:12:81:529:129:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:acceptance rate of best offers, which ignores the implicit rejection of offers received along with the best offer.
41:1308:93264:12:12:81:529:117:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:While the acceptance rate of all offers is lower for the employed, so is the acceptance rate of the unemployed
41:1309:93374:12:12:81:529:105:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:and thus the model does no better in matching the acceptance rate of the employed.  We believe that targeting
41:1310:93483:12:12:81:529:93:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:the acceptance rate of best offers as our baseline is consistent with using the fraction receiving at least one offer
41:1311:93600:12:0:81:325:81:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:in a given month as our measure of the job offer probability.
42:1312:93663:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed  to  0.60,  from  0.339,  which  implies  the  unemployment-to-employment  transition
42:1313:93759:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rate of 0.32 targeted by Hornstein, Krusell, and Violante (2011).  The results indicate a flow
42:1314:93853:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:value  of  unemployment  of  0.16  and  0.58  for  the  two  versions  of  our  model  with  unobserved
42:1315:93956:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:worker heterogeneity that preclude and allow for differing wage offer distributions between the
42:1316:94051:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed and unemployed, respectively.  Thus, our resolution of the puzzle holds for the full
42:1317:94144:25:25:81:182:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:version of our model.
42:1318:94165:25:25:81:530:559:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f4:B:Targeting raw moments instead of residualized differences:Since our model does not
42:1319:94247:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:feature observed heterogeneity such as education and gender, our baseline calibration targets
42:1320:94340:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:data moments that control for observable worker and job characteristics. As a robustness check,
42:1321:94435:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we also consider a calibration where we target the raw, unconditional moments in our data.  In
42:1322:94529:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this case, the empirical wage offer gap between the employed and unemployed is 0.40.  While
42:1323:94620:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the importance of heterogeneity obviously rises relative to our baseline in this case, nearly half
42:1324:94718:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the wage offer differential remains unexplained by either worker heterogeneity or censoring
42:1325:94812:25:25:81:223:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the wage offer distribution.
42:1326:94843:25:25:98:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:In  summary,  we  take  from  our  calibration  exercise  that  models  with  endogenous  search
42:1327:94939:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effort and wage offer distributions that differ across labor force status do better at fitting the
42:1328:95037:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:relevant facts in our survey and in particular the acceptance rate of the employed, which was not
42:1329:95134:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:targeted in the calibration.  The models also produce reasonable flow values of unemployment.
42:1330:95227:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:While there is substantial disagreement in the literature on the exact value of the flow value,
42:1331:95322:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ranging from 0.4 (Shimer, 2005) to near unity (Hagedorn and Manovskii, 2008), it is difficult
42:1332:95415:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to rationalize a value below 0.4.  Our full version of the model with endogenous search effort,
42:1333:95510:25:36:81:504:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:worker-heterogeneity and differing wage offer distributions produces a flow value of 0.74.
42:1334:95600:36:35:81:253:149:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:5    Concluding Remarks
42:1335:95623:35:25:81:529:114:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:In  this  paper,  we  document  new  facts  about  the  search  effort  and  search  outcomes  of  both
42:1336:95726:25:0:81:529:89:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed and non-employed workers.  We find that search among the employed is pervasive
43:1337:95815:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and  more  efficient.   Moreover,  among  the  employed,  we  find  a  strong  correlation  of  search
43:1338:95917:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:outcomes  and  effort,  with  those  searching  having  a  five  times  higher  chance  of  receiving  at
43:1339:96022:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:least  one  offer  over  the  period  of  a  month.   Nevertheless,  a  sizable  fraction  of  job  offers  go
43:1340:96132:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to  employed  workers  not  even  looking  for  work,  underscoring  the  importance  of  unsolicited
43:1341:96233:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employer  contacts  in  the  job  search  process.   We  also  find  that  the  employed  are  not  only
43:1342:96337:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:more efficient in their search, but they also tend to receive and accept better job offers.  The
43:1343:96433:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:differences in search outcomes persist even after controlling for observable characteristics of the
43:1344:96532:25:25:81:156:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:worker and job.
43:1345:96547:25:25:98:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We apply our results to an on-the-job search framework with endogenous search effort and
43:1346:96635:25:25:81:530:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unobservedex anteworker heterogeneity, also accounting for the many distinct aspects of our
43:1347:96726:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:data such as unsolicited offers, censoring, and heterogeneity in search efficiency by labor force
43:1348:96823:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status.   We  find  that  unobserved  heterogeneity  explains  only  28  percent  of  the  offered  wage
43:1349:96927:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:differential between the employed and the unemployed.  Extending our model by allowing for
43:1350:97017:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:differences in wage offer distributions provides a remarkably good fit to the data, generating
43:1351:97111:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:enough wage dispersion and a reasonable flow value of unemployment.  Our empirical findings
43:1352:97202:25:25:81:530:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:thus constitute an important piece in the resolution of thefrictional wage dispersion puzzlein
43:1353:97296:25:25:81:249:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the search and matching literature.
43:1354:97331:25:25:98:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:An  open  question  remains  as  to  why  wage  offers  appear  to  be  of  better  quality  for  the
43:1355:97432:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed compared to the non-employed even after controlling for unobserved heterogeneity
43:1356:97521:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and the censoring of job offers.  One can think of a variety of possible reasons.  Some are more
43:1357:97617:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:familiar  to  the  literature  that  has  studied  wages,  job  loss,  and  unemployment,  while  others
43:1358:97721:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are more specific to the job search process.  While we leave a thorough evaluation of different
43:1359:97816:24:25:81:382:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:mechanisms to future research, we briefly discuss these factors.
43:1360:97880:25:25:98:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:One possibility is that human capital depreciates during periods of non-employment.  In
43:1361:97967:25:25:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:this case,  the employed and non-employed may have a similar wage (and potentially similar
43:1362:98057:25:0:81:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:skill  levels)  when  they  separate  from  their  previous  job,  but  the  skills  of  the  non-employed
44:1363:98165:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:depreciate, leading them to have lower-quality job offers, on average.  In this case, accounting
44:1364:98261:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for the work history of the employed and non-employed would reduce the gap.  We show that
44:1365:98350:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:controlling  for  the  previous  five-year  work  history  (specifically,  the  fraction  of  the  prior  five
44:1366:98460:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:years spent employed, unemployed, etc.)  only reduces the wage gap from 0.250 to 0.205.  This
44:1367:98553:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:suggests that human capital depreciation can explain only a small fraction of the wage offer
44:1368:98645:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:gap.  As discussed in the previous section, questions about five-year work histories do a good
44:1369:98739:25:25:81:381:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job at capturing unobserved differences in worker productivity.
44:1370:98802:25:25:98:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The presence of bargaining and counter-offers are another way the search process can affect
44:1371:98893:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the wage offer gap.  We find that 39 percent of offers to the employed, while only 24 percent of
44:1372:98989:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offers to the non-employed, involved some bargaining between the individual and the potential
44:1373:99082:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employer.   In  general,  a  greater  propensity  to  bargain  with  the  potential  employer  should
44:1374:99183:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:increase the reported wage offer, all else equal.  Moreover, 14 percent of the employed with an
44:1375:99278:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:outside offer received some form of counter-offer from their current employer.  While the latter
44:1376:99374:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:estimate  falls  clearly  short  of  the  rate  of  counter-offers  in  models  such  as  Postel-Vinay  and
44:1377:99481:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Robin (2002) and Cahuc, Postel-Vinay, and Robin (2006), it is possible that the threat of such
44:1378:99575:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:counter-offers raises the mean wage offers for the employed even when no such offer occurs in
44:1379:99668:25:25:81:138:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:equilibrium.
44:1380:99680:25:25:98:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:The employed may have access to more effective job search networks that provide better job
44:1381:99770:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offers (see, for example, Arbex, O’Dey, and Wiczer, 2016).  Our empirical analysis shows that
44:1382:99863:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the employed are about twice as likely to have received an offer through an unsolicited contact
44:1383:99958:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:as  the  non-employed.   If  these  informal  offers  represent  higher-quality  jobs,  then  the  higher
44:1384:100063:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:incidence of unsolicited offers should also contribute to the wage offer gap.  However, we find
44:1385:100158:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that unsolicited offers have lower wages, on average, and that there is no significant difference
44:1386:100255:25:25:81:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:between the unsolicited offers of the employed and non-employed.  If anything, the employed
44:1387:100346:25:25:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:receive slightly lower wage offers from unsolicited contacts than the non-employed,  with our
44:1388:100439:25:0:81:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:estimates indicating that the log wage offer of the employed is a statistically-insignificant 0.023
45:1389:100540:0:25:81:109:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:lower.
45:1390:100546:25:25:98:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Finally, our evidence is also consistent with an implicit penalty for job seeking while unem-
45:1391:100639:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ployed, in line with the results of Kroft, Lange, and Notowidigdo (2013).  Such a penalty could
45:1392:100734:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:be due to either explicit discrimination or a perceived signal about a worker’s unobserved pro-
45:1393:100829:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:ductivity that employers infer from their employment status (as in Gibbons and Katz, 1991).
45:1394:100920:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:It is also possible that it is the result of firms discriminating against the unemployed to take
45:1395:101016:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:advantage  of  their  lower  reservation  wages  (as  in  Carrillo-Tudela,  2009).   Regardless  of  its
45:1396:101120:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:source, any such penalty on job search while unemployed would imply that the employed and
45:1397:101209:25:0:81:352:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed draw from differing wage offer distributions.

51:1495:108169:0:27:81:475:709:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:A    Comparison of SCE Labor Survey to External Data
51:1496:108221:27:32:81:396:682:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:A.1    Results using CPS Definition of Unemployment
51:1497:108272:32:24:81:529:650:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:As  we  note  in  the  main  text,  our  survey  allows  for  a  broader  measure  of  job  search  among
51:1498:108377:24:25:81:529:626:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the non-employed than what is possible using the Current Population Survey (CPS). The CPS
51:1499:108466:25:25:81:529:601:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:counts the unemployed as those non-employed individuals who either were on temporary layoff
51:1500:108557:25:25:81:529:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or had actively searched within the last four weeks and were available for work.  We use the
51:1501:108649:25:25:81:529:551:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:same definition, but unlike the CPS, we ask all individuals if they had searched for work.  The
51:1502:108744:25:25:81:529:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:difference in scope between the two surveys is that the CPS does not follow up with certain
51:1503:108835:25:25:81:529:501:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:non-employed individuals (predominantly the retired and disabled), who report that they either
51:1504:108929:25:25:81:379:476:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:do not want work or cannot work, to ask if they had searched.
51:1505:108990:25:25:98:529:451:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Our  survey  suggests  that  many  of  these  individuals  actively  search  and  are  available  for
51:1506:109091:25:25:81:529:426:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:work.  Table A1 shows that they represent just over 12 percent of those counted as out of the
51:1507:109184:25:25:81:529:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:labor force under the CPS definition.  As Table 1 in the main text shows, including these indi-
51:1508:109279:25:24:81:529:376:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:viduals in our job search measure increases the measured unemployment rate by 2.7 percentage
51:1509:109371:24:25:81:529:352:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:points.  Further analysis (not reported here) suggests that the majority of the difference is due
51:1510:109468:25:25:81:322:327:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to retired individuals seeking only part-time work.
51:1511:109519:25:25:98:529:302:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Tables A1, A2, and A3 replicate our job search analysis using the CPS scope and definition
51:1512:109609:25:25:81:529:277:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of unemployment.  Using the SCE labor survey,  this counts those non-employed who do not
51:1513:109697:25:25:81:529:252:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:explicitly report wanting work as out of the labor force, regardless of whether they later report
51:1514:109794:25:25:81:529:227:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that they actively searched and are available.  The tables correspond to Tables 2, 3, and 4 in
51:1515:109888:25:25:81:529:202:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the main text, though we only report the replicated results of Tables 3 and 4 by labor force
51:1516:109980:25:25:81:529:177:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status at the time of the survey since the difference in unemployment definition only matters
51:1517:110073:25:25:81:529:152:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for this period.  We determine labor force status in the previous month using responses from a
51:1518:110167:25:25:81:529:127:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:variety of other survey questions that do not directly correspond to the CPS definition.  Note
51:1519:110261:25:24:81:529:102:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:also that the results for the employed (regardless of whether they actively searched for work)
51:1520:110355:24:0:81:254:78:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are the same under both definitions.
52:1521:110393:0:25:98:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Table A1 shows that there is a sizable fraction (12 percent) of those out of the labor force
52:1522:110485:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:actively searching and available for work.  By definition, none of these individuals are out of
52:1523:110580:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  labor  force  using  the  job  search  measure  of  unemployment.   A  similar  fraction  of  those
52:1524:110684:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:out of the labor force sent at least one application in the prior four weeks or spent some time
52:1525:110779:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:searching in the previous seven days.  Unlike the unemployed under the CPS definition, about
52:1526:110871:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:half of those actively searching from out of the labor force are only seeking part-time work.
52:1527:110964:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Conditional on actively searching, just under 10 percent are looking for work similar to their
52:1528:111058:25:25:81:158:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:most recent job.
52:1529:111074:25:25:98:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Tables A2 and A3 show that moving from our job search measure to the CPS measure of
52:1530:111157:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployment has only a minor effect on our estimates for the intensive margin of search effort
52:1531:111252:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and search outcomes for the unemployed.  The CPS definition implies a somewhat higher level
52:1532:111343:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of search effort.  The number of applications rises by 19 percent, to 9.65 per month, and time
52:1533:111437:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:spent searching rises by 30 percent, to 10.96 hours per week.  The number of employer contacts,
52:1534:111532:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job interviews, and job offers received all rise somewhat as well, though the differences between
52:1535:111629:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the estimates in Table A3 and Table 4 in the main text are not statistically significant.  The job
52:1536:111727:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search definition and the CPS definition of employment also imply similar ratios of employer
52:1537:111819:25:25:81:372:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:contacts per application and mean job offers per application.
52:1538:111880:25:25:98:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Finally, note that the definitions used here have no bearing on our model calibration since
52:1539:111971:25:30:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:it uses job search effort and outcome estimates based on labor force status in the prior month.
52:1540:112066:30:32:81:391:230:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:A.2    Search Effort Estimates in the SCE and ATUS
52:1541:112116:32:25:81:529:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:In this section, we compare our estimates of search effort, specifically the time spent searching
52:1542:112213:25:25:81:529:173:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for work, to the comparable measure from the time diaries of the American Time Use Survey
52:1543:112302:25:25:81:529:148:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:(ATUS). We use the ATUS data as a comparison because existing measures of search effort are
52:1544:112393:25:25:81:386:123:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rare, particularly when one wants to measure on-the-job search.
52:1545:112456:25:25:98:529:98:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Table A4 reports the results of our comparison.  We focus on individuals age 18-64 in both
52:1546:112546:25:0:81:529:73:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:surveys and report average time spent searching for work by broad labor force status (employed,

55:1650:116345:15:15:87:535:349:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:?:Use  Survey  (top  panel)  and  the  SCE  labor  survey  (bottom  panel),  for  all  individuals  aged
55:1651:116447:15:15:87:535:334:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:?:18-64, by labor force status.  The SCE estimates use the BLS definition of unemployment for
55:1652:116538:15:15:87:535:319:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:?:determining  labor  force  status.   Standard  errors  are  in  parentheses.   We  do  not  report  the
55:1653:116641:15:35:87:347:304:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:?:standard errors for the ATUS as they were very small.
55:1654:116694:35:25:81:530:269:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed, or out of the labor force).41To maintain consistency across the surveys, we use
55:1655:116785:25:25:81:529:244:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the CPS definition of unemployment described above for the SCE data.  There are differences
55:1656:116876:25:25:81:529:219:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:between the frequencies over which each survey measures search time, which we cannot control
55:1657:116968:25:25:81:529:194:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for.   The  ATUS  measures  search  time  for  the  previous  day  using  a  detailed  time  diary  of
55:1658:117070:25:25:81:529:169:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:activities, while the SCE asks respondents the number of hours they spent searching for work
55:1659:117162:25:24:81:220:144:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:over the previous seven days.
55:1660:117191:24:25:98:529:120:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We find notable differences between our estimates of time spent searching for work in the
55:1661:117280:25:16:81:529:95:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:SCE labor supplement and the estimates from the ATUS, with the SCE estimates implying
55:1662:117365:16:0:90:361:79:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:?:41See also Mueller (2010) for similar statistics for an earlier period.
56:1663:117438:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:much  more  time  spent  searching  for  work  overall,  but  less  time  spent  searching  in  terms  of
56:1664:117543:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:a  daily  average,  when  we  condition  on  individuals  who  reported  positive  search  time.   We
56:1665:117644:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:believe  that  this  is  likely  because  of  both  measurement  differences  between  the  surveys  and
56:1666:117748:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  nature  of  search.   Aside  from  the  obvious  difference  between  the  ATUS  and  SCE  data
56:1667:117848:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that  the  former  measures  search  for  a  single  day  and  the  SCE  over  one  week,  the  ATUS
56:1668:117948:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:time diary also likely understates the time spent searching for work because individuals only
56:1669:118041:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:report their primary activity in the diary.  Thus,  if an individual is literally searching while
56:1670:118138:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on the job, it will likely show up as work time rather than search time.  The daily data also
56:1671:118231:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:likely understate search effort because, as the comparison of the two surveys suggests, search
56:1672:118325:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:effort is discrete and intermittent.  The daily data imply that only 0.5 percent of the employed
56:1673:118421:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:reported any time spent searching on a given day, yet we find that 20.5 percent of the employed
56:1674:118516:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:reported searching within the last seven days, and in Table 2 of the main text, we find that
56:1675:118608:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:23.3 percent of the employed report actively searching within the past four weeks.  Even among
56:1676:118702:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the  unemployed,  who  are  defined  as  actively  looking  for  work,  the  ATUS  estimates  suggest
56:1677:118803:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that only 20.2 percent looked for work on the previous day, while the SCE estimates suggest
56:1678:118894:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that 93.5 percent searched within the last seven days.  If we condition our estimates on those
56:1679:118988:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:who reported positive search time, and compare the daily average time from the SCE to the
56:1680:119077:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:reported daily time spent searching in the ATUS, we get that the SCE estimates suggest lower
56:1681:119169:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:search effort per day, on average.  Again, this is likely evidence that search is intermittent, with
56:1682:119269:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:individuals searching for some time on several days during a week than a set amount each day.
56:1683:119362:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Consequently, when we couple our evidence from the SCE data with the previous research on
56:1684:119451:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on-the-job search mentioned in the main text (e.g., Black, 1980; Blau and Robins, 1990), we
56:1685:119542:24:25:81:529:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:feel that our estimates of search effort provide a more comprehensive and reliable measure than
56:1686:119637:25:0:81:132:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the ATUS.
57:1687:119648:0:27:81:489:709:14:g_d0_f3:14:g_d0_f3:C:B    Measuring Labor Force Status in the Previous Month
57:1688:119703:27:32:81:529:682:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:F:B.1    Prior Month’s Labor Force Status Based on the SCE Labor Supplement
57:1689:119776:32:24:81:529:650:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We derive a labor force status for individuals four weeks prior to their survey interview using a
57:1690:119873:24:25:81:529:626:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:range of survey responses from the SCE labor supplement.  We use the prior month’s labor force
57:1691:119967:25:25:81:529:601:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status in our model calibration because it treats the search effort and offer arrivals reported in
57:1692:120065:25:25:81:436:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the survey as subsequent outcomes based on this initial labor force status.
57:1693:120140:25:25:98:529:551:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:This  appendix  details  our  methodology  for  determining  labor  force  status  in  the  prior
57:1694:120237:25:25:81:529:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:month and evaluates our measure along several comparable dimensions.  To determine labor
57:1695:120325:25:25:81:529:501:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:force status in the prior month, we first check to see if an individual received an offer in the
57:1696:120421:25:25:81:529:476:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:four weeks prior to the survey.  If so, we assign them their labor force status at the time they
57:1697:120517:25:25:81:529:451:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:received their job offer (employed or non-employed).  This approach assumes that labor force
57:1698:120609:25:25:81:529:426:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:status did not change between the time they received their job offer and four weeks prior to
57:1699:120701:25:25:81:529:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the labor supplement survey.  We feel that this is a reasonable assumption given the relatively
57:1700:120796:25:24:81:529:376:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:short time interval.  In the 2014 and 2015 waves of the survey, we have additional information
57:1701:120890:24:25:81:529:352:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:on whether an individual was actively searching at the time they received their offer.  If so, we
57:1702:120987:25:25:81:529:327:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:count them as unemployed, and if not, we count them as out of the labor force.  For those in
57:1703:121079:25:25:81:529:302:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the 2013 wave, we have to make some modest assumptions to determine whether someone was
57:1704:121166:25:25:81:529:277:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:unemployed or out of the labor force.  If an individual who was non-employed at the time of the
57:1705:121261:25:25:81:529:252:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:job offer was employed at the time of the 2013 labor survey, we assume that they were actively
57:1706:121355:25:25:81:529:227:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:searching and count them as unemployed.  If they were unemployed at the time of the survey
57:1707:121445:25:25:81:529:202:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and have been searching for over four weeks, we also count them as unemployed.  Otherwise,
57:1708:121535:25:25:81:382:177:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we count them as out of the labor force in the previous month.
57:1709:121597:25:25:98:529:152:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:For  the  remaining  individuals  (who  are  the  vast  majority  of  respondents),  we  determine
57:1710:121695:25:25:81:529:127:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:their prior month’s labor force status starting with their labor force status at the time of the
57:1711:121791:25:24:81:529:102:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:survey.  If an individual did not receive a job offer in the last four weeks but was employed
57:1712:121884:24:0:81:529:78:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:at the  time of the  survey,  we determine  their prior month’s  labor  force status as  follows:  if
58:1713:121987:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:they report that their current job tenure is at least one month, or if they report tenure of less
58:1714:122084:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:than a month but with less than two weeks between jobs, we count them as employed in the
58:1715:122172:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:previous month.  Otherwise, we assume that these individuals were actively searching for work
58:1716:122265:25:25:81:342:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and count them as unemployed in the previous month.
58:1717:122316:25:25:98:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:If an individual was unemployed at the time of the survey and did not receive an offer in the
58:1718:122409:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:last four weeks, we count them as employed in the previous month if they were on temporary
58:1719:122499:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:layoff  for  less  than  one  month  or  if  their  current  non-employment  spell  was  one  month  or
58:1720:122602:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:less.  We count them as unemployed in the prior month if they were on temporary layoff for
58:1721:122692:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:more than one month or if they report actively searching for work for more than one month.
58:1722:122782:25:25:81:328:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Otherwise, we count them as out of the labor force.
58:1723:122833:25:24:98:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Finally,  if  an  individual  was  out  of  the  labor  force  at  the  time  of  the  survey  and  did
58:1724:122936:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:not receive an offer in the last four weeks,  we count them as employed if their current non-
58:1725:123029:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employment spell is one month or less.  We count them as unemployed if they report actively
58:1726:123120:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:searching for more than one month and they are not currently disabled.  Otherwise, we count
58:1727:123211:25:25:81:227:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:them as out of the labor force.
58:1728:123242:25:25:98:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Evaluation of this approach suggests that our methodology produces a sensible measure of
58:1729:123330:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the prior month’s labor force status along several dimensions.  First, our estimates imply an
58:1730:123423:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employment-to-population ratio of 0.750,  an unemployment rate of 6.3 percent,  and a labor
58:1731:123514:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:force participation rate of 80.0 percent.  All are roughly comparable to the CPS estimates and
58:1732:123608:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the SCE labor supplement estimates (using the BLS definition of unemployment) in Table 1
58:1733:123696:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  the  main  text.   The  unemployment  rate  in  the  previous  month  is  likely  somewhat  higher
58:1734:123798:25:24:81:529:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:because of our assumption that all those who became employed during the month but did not
58:1735:123887:24:25:81:308:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:report a job offer were unemployed when hired.
58:1736:123933:25:25:98:529:136:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Second, Table B1 reports labor force transition rates for two data sources.  The first source
58:1737:124026:25:25:81:529:111:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:is the SCE labor supplement, which estimates the transition rates using our measure of labor
58:1738:124118:25:0:81:529:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:force status in the prior month and labor force status at the time of survey (using the CPS
59:1739:124211:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:definition described in Appendix A). The second source is the monthly CPS, which measures
59:1740:124300:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the transition rates between September and October of each year (2013-15) for individuals in
59:1741:124392:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the survey during both months.  The transition rates for the SCE are generally very comparable
59:1742:124486:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to the transition rates for the CPS. The job-separation rates into unemployment and out of
59:1743:124576:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the labor force are nearly identical.  The SCE labor supplement has a slightly lower job-finding
59:1744:124672:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:rate of the unemployed and a notably lower job-finding rate for those out of the labor force.
59:1745:124765:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Transitions between unemployment and being out of the labor force are roughly comparable
59:1746:124853:25:24:81:201:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:between the two surveys.

59:1762:125577:12:12:81:506:242:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:F:status and uses the CPS definition of unemployment for labor force status at the time of the survey.  The

59:1765:125807:39:32:81:491:179:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:B.2    Prior Month’s Labor Force Status Based on Monthly SCE Data
59:1766:125872:32:25:81:529:147:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:Another way to test the validity of our estimates of labor force status in the prior month is
59:1767:125965:25:25:81:529:122:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to  compare  it  to  results  based  on  labor  force  status  for  individuals  in  the  regular,  monthly
59:1768:126072:25:25:81:529:97:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:SCE for the previous month.  The monthly SCE data’s measure of labor force status in the
59:1769:126160:25:0:81:529:72:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:previous month is generally not consistent with the timing of the SCE labor supplement because
60:1770:126256:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:individuals  may  respond  to  the  labor  supplement  anywhere  from  a  few  days  to  nearly  two
60:1771:126356:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:months after their most recent monthly SCE interview.  To deal with this, we assign a prior
60:1772:126447:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:month’s labor force status to individuals in the labor supplement based on the timing between
60:1773:126540:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the supplement and their September SCE interview.  If the gap between interviews is 22 days
60:1774:126631:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or  more,  we  use  their  September  labor  force  status.   If  the  gap  is  21  days  or  less,  or  if  the
60:1775:126743:25:25:81:405:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:September data are missing, we use their August labor force status.
60:1776:126810:25:25:98:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Table B2 replicates the bottom panels of Tables 3 and 4 of the main text using the prior
60:1777:126898:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:month’s labor force status measure derived from the monthly SCE data.  The table shows that
60:1778:126989:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the estimates are very similar to those estimated using our prior month’s labor force status
60:1779:127081:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:measure derived from the labor supplement.  Some minor exceptions exist for the unemployed.
60:1780:127172:25:24:81:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:For  example,  their  application  and  offer  rates  are  somewhat  lower  using  the  monthly  SCE
60:1781:127272:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:measure  of  prior  month’s  labor  force  status.   Otherwise,  the  two  measures  produce  nearly

64:1898:133810:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:or timing of the measurement of labor force status.  The search effort of the employed relative
64:1899:133905:25:25:81:529:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to the unemployed, ignoring search for additional work (our preferred measure of relative effort
64:1900:134001:25:24:81:354:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for our model calibration) falls slightly from 0.91 to 0.86.
64:1901:134061:24:25:98:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Tables C2 and C3 show that search outcomes and acceptance rates also change little after
64:1902:134149:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:controlling for observables.  If we ignore the effects of censoring of the wage offer distribution,
64:1903:134248:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we can infer the relative search efficiency of the employed to the unemployed directly from the
64:1904:134343:25:25:81:530:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:data asλi(s) =αi+βis.  Using the unconditional estimates from Table 4 in the main text
64:1905:134429:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:suggests that relative efficiency measured this way is about 2.2 (compared to nearly three in
64:1906:134522:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the calibrated model).  If we were to instead use the estimates from Table C2, the estimates
64:1907:134614:25:25:81:455:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:suggest that the relative efficiency under this method is slightly higher, at 2.5.

69:2017:141827:27:32:81:368:682:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:C:D.1    Results for Alternative Model Calibrations
69:2018:141876:32:24:81:529:650:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:This subsection reports the results of our robustness exercises that we detail in the main text.
69:2019:141972:24:25:81:529:626:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:We report the results for the two versions of our model that include unobserved worker hetero-
69:2020:142066:25:25:81:530:601:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:geneity.  The first version assumes anex anteidentical wage offer distribution for the employed
69:2021:142161:25:25:81:529:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:and  unemployed  (corresponding  to  column  3  of  Table  9),  while  the  second  version  assumes
69:2022:142261:25:25:81:529:551:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:that, additionally, the wage offer distribution differs by employment status (corresponding to
69:2023:142355:25:25:81:183:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:column 4 of Table 9).
69:2024:142376:25:25:98:529:501:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:Tables D1 and D2 report our results.  The baseline model in column (1) refers to the model
69:2025:142466:25:25:81:529:476:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:with identical wage offer distributions in Table D1 (corresponding to column (3) in Table 9 of the
69:2026:142564:25:25:81:529:451:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:main text) and to the model with differing wage offer distributions in Table D2 (corresponding
69:2027:142658:25:25:81:529:426:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:to column (4) in Table 9 of the main text).  Columns (2)-(6) in each table report the results
69:2028:142751:25:25:81:529:401:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  our  five  robustness  exercises  described  in  the  main  text.   Column  (2)  reports  the  results
69:2029:142857:25:24:81:529:376:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of our alternative calibration for the censoring of wage offers;  column (3) reports the results
69:2030:142953:24:25:81:529:352:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of  increasing  the  dispersion  of  wage  offers;  column  (4)  reports  the  results  of  our  alternative
69:2031:143061:25:25:81:529:327:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:calibration of the curvature of the search cost function; column (5) reports the results of our
69:2032:143156:25:25:81:529:302:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:alternative calibration of the job-finding rate; and column (6) reports the results of our use of
69:2033:143253:25:25:81:529:277:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the unconditional data moments in the calibration.  We refer the reader to the main text for
69:2034:143345:25:25:81:529:252:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the summaries of these results.  Robustness results for the remaining two versions of our model
69:2035:143440:25:31:81:209:227:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:are available upon request.

72:2141:148717:0:25:81:529:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:asking what fraction of that time was spent employed, unemployed, in school, or otherwise out
72:2142:148810:25:25:81:529:684:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:of the labor force. In Table 10 of the main text, we report the results of including two versions of
72:2143:148910:25:25:81:529:659:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:these controls, in addition to the controls for observable worker and job characteristics reported
72:2144:149008:25:25:81:529:634:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:in Table 7.  We use two versions of work history.  The first version controls for employment
72:2145:149100:25:25:81:529:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:history by adding the fraction of the last five years spent employed to the existing controls for
72:2146:149197:25:25:81:529:584:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:observables.  The first column of Table 10 shows that this reduces the estimated (log) wage
72:2147:149288:25:25:81:529:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:offer gap between the employed and non-employed from 0.250 to 0.222.  The second version
72:2148:149376:25:25:81:529:534:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:controls for labor force history by additionally adding the fraction of the last five years spent
72:2149:149473:25:25:81:529:509:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:as either unemployed or as a student (with the share spent otherwise out of the labor force as
72:2150:149567:25:25:81:524:484:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:the excluded category).  Table 10 shows that this further reduces the estimated gap to 0.205.
72:2151:149660:25:24:98:529:459:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A:We can simulate our model to generate what our calibration implies about how the controls
72:2152:149749:24:25:81:529:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for work history should affect our estimated wage gap.  To this end, we simulated data from the
72:2153:149844:25:25:81:529:410:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:model and then performed the same regressions as in the data.  The second column of Table
72:2154:149933:25:25:81:529:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:10 reports our simulation results.  The model is calibrated to match the estimated wage offer
72:2155:150026:25:25:81:529:360:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:gap controlling for observables only by construction.  The simulation suggests that controlling
72:2156:150121:25:25:81:529:335:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:for work history should reduce the gap from 0.25 to 0.21 if we only control for the time spent
72:2157:150215:25:25:81:529:310:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:employed over the last five years.  The model-implied gaps are very similar to the gaps that
72:2158:150307:25:25:81:529:285:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:we  estimate  from  the  data.   As  discussed  in  the  text,  the  similarity  of  results  is  remarkable
72:2159:150415:25:25:81:529:260:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:as  the  model  calibration  exploits  an  independent  source  of  variation  in  prior  wages  rather
72:2160:150518:25:25:81:529:235:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:than employment histories.  This, thus, suggests that the question about five-year employment
72:2161:150611:25:25:81:529:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:history captures well the unobserved heterogeneity that is relevant for the empirically observed
72:2162:150707:25:0:81:178:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:B:wage offer premium.
